# E
## e hoa ma
 
 <i>exclam.</i> my friend (as form of address) [ORIGIN: Maori, -] (See [ehoa](../E#ehoa))

>  We have concerns e hoa ma. <i>(May 19, 2006,Whakatane Beacon,10</i>)

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> E hoa ma, Cookie, why do you frown? [fn] E hoa ma, my friend. Ibid. 4/6/297 ` E hoa ma' - Friend of mine - `that is what I want my pigs to do for me. I have plenty of fern-root too.' <i>(1881,Poenamo,Bk2/3/71</i>)



## e-to-ki
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [toki](../T#toki))



## e-tootoo
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [tutu](../T#tutu))



## EA
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Expatriate Assignment

>  The paper [by Auckland professor Kerr Inkson] compared New Zealanders who went on OE - and found their own jobs while they were away - with those who were sent by their employers for an expatriate assignment (EA). <i>(Sep 13, 1997,Dominion,1</i>)



## EAB
 
 <i>abbr.</i> External Assessments Bureau

>  There's an EAB [External Assessments Bureau] report, which basically has the same material in it as the stuff that came out of Defence. <i>(May 12, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),19</i>)



## eagle
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Sarah.. had already.. checked out via mates and friends of mates exactly what she'd need should the eagle land before sending in her entry. <i>(Jul 31, 2003,Contact (Wgtn),4</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> The company paying employees. The eagle shits (tomorrow), it's payday (tomorrow).

>  <b>MORRIESON</b> `If that's on the cards, let's get few flagons,' said Entwhistle quickly. `After all the eagle shits tomorrow' This was freezing workers' slang for pay-day. <i>Ibid.</i> 119 Here was the siding to the sprawling empire of the Freezing Works, the `Eagle' as the men called it - ... <i>(1976,Pallet on the Floor,29</i>)



## eagle ray
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [ray](../R#ray))

>  Authorities state its nomenclature as the eagle ray, or sea devil, and it is sometimes called the "devil fish." <i>(Jan 1, 1908,Otago Witness,76</i>)



## eaglewist
 
 <i>n.</i> edelweiss [(musterers')]

>  <b>McLEOD</b> In spite of their life they [musterers] are usually ignorant of natural history; the alpine plants all round them they seldom know by name; even the snowy eidelweiss [sic] they know, if at all, as `eaglewist.' <i>(1951,High Country,44</i>)



## ear shell
 
 <i>n.</i> (obsolete) = paua (shell) [-] (See [sea-ear](../S#sea-ear))

>  Its whole length does not exceed two feet, ending in the representation of a face, which is a piece of carving exhibiting a most preposterous group of features - two large Saucer-eyes formed of broad rings of Ear-shell ... [Footnote: the shell of a `sea ear', the paua.] <i>(Oct 11, 1769,Cook's Journals,1/574</i>) [NOTES: W.B. MonkhouseCheck whether date ll or 6.]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We landed & collected vast numbers of Ear-Shells of a very large Size on the Shore between the Stones. <i>(Apr 9, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/250</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> [The canoe] had a carved head ... with eyes made of round pieces of ear-shell, which somewhat resembled mother of pearl. <i>(1777,Voyage Round the World,1/132</i>)

>  In the centre of this circle [on Chatham Island] was the mark of a fire place, and a great number of Fish Shells lay about, particularly the Earshell. <i>(Nov 29, 1791,Journal of the 'Chatham'</i>) [NOTES: in 1908McNab Historical Records 2/505]

>  ... a great number of Fish Shells lay about [the Chatham Islands fireplace], particularly the Earshells - <i>(Nov 1791,Journal of Voyage in HMS 'Chatham',60</i>) [NOTES: 1791 Nov E.Bell Journal of Voyage in H.M.S. `Chatham' (Vancouver expedition) (MS ATB)]

>  <b>MURRY</b> ... the Falls about Facill Harbour being dry, I went to Earshell Cove, and fill'd the casks ... <i>(Oct 24, 1795,Journal</i>) [NOTES: in 1908 McNab Historical Records 2/526]

>  <b>SAVAGE</b> The hooks are formed of the outer rim of the ear-shell, well polished, and barbed at the extremity. <i>(1807,New Zealand (various authors/titles),58</i>)

>  <b>TANCRED</b> Shell, called from its shape the ear-shell ... They are sought for the mother-of-pearl with which they are lined under the name of paua-shells. <i>(1856,Natural History of Canterbury,16</i>)

>  The ear-shell was used by the Maoris in the manufacture of fish-hooks. <i>(1872,TrNZI,367</i>) [NOTES: Kirk]



## ear-piece
 
 <i>n.</i> The detached scalp of a sheep including the ears. [(freezing works)]

>  [The chopper also scalps them which gets taken off as the ear-piece, and goes down to the pie-house, on to the pie-heap where it rots and is plucked by the pie-pickers. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Feilding Freezing Works terms p.c. Colin Gordon]



## earbash
 
 <i>v.</i> To talk long and hard at; to bore with words; to nag; talk in ordinately to someone

>  <b>CROSS</b> We went on ear-bashing each other like that for the next hour or so. <i>(1957,God Boy, The,81</i>)

>  <b>McCALLUM</b> `How to Make Friends Without Ear-bashing Them.' ... [25] ... For once in her life she don't [sic] earbash me. <i>(1963,Half-Gallon Jar,17</i>)

>  <b>SYDER & HODGETTS</b> To talk constantly, to the extent of wearying the listener. .in 4;`I could see poor Henry suffering in silence. Jim was earbashing him nearly all the evening - Jim never gives the other fellow a chance to open his mouth, you can't get a word in edgeways.' <i>(1962,Australian & NZ English [TS],302</i>)

>  <b>FRANCES</b> `Now, after ear bashing you like that, the least I can do is take you out to lunch.' <i>(1964,Johnny Rapana,183</i>)

>  ear-bash v. NZ & Austral. sl. talk inordinately (to); ear-basher n. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,235</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 152]]

>  <b>McGILL</b> earbash to talk, perhaps to harangue; eg `Give him half a chance and he'll earbash you all night.' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,42</i>)



## earbashing
 
 <i>n.</i> a lengthy talking-to; a talking at length. [1951 16+ M 26 Wellington H.S. 23 (in UK dictionaries with no regional label TD)]

>  the [sic] phys ed teacher complains that a whole period of ear-bashing ... cannot make 14 yr old boys dance with girls. <i>(1973,Islands,6/385</i>) [NOTES: Body (Diary of a School Music Teacher)]

>  <b>LEE</b> I'm too weak to stand up to the earbashing. <i>(1976,Soldier,3</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> Ear Bashing. Talking to excess. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)



## earina
 
 <i>n.</i> orchid q.v., esp. Easter orchid.

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Earina autumnalis </i> fragrant earina. <i>(1908,AJHR,C-14</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Survey Waipoua Kauri Forest]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Earina suaveoleus </i> ... <i>Earina mucronata </i> <i>(1906,Plants of NZ</i>)

>  <i>Earina mucronata </i> pointed-leaved orchid. <i>(1908,AJHR,C-14</i>) [NOTES: Report on a Botanical Survey of Waipoua Kauri Forest]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Earina autumnalis </i> sweet-scented earina, <i>(1909,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: Report on a Botanical Survey of Stewart Island]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Fragrant Earina - raupeka, <i>Earina autumnalis </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

>  <i>Leucogenes grandiceps </i> <i>(1885,NZ Country Journal</i>) [NOTES: Vol I 1877-XXII 1898 (And) (Canterbury A & P Ass.)]



## early
 
 <i>adj.</i> used of the pioneering period of Pakeha settlement in NZ; esp. early settler etc. [More cits needed.]

>  <b>BLAKISTON</b> ... I had known the Orari Gorge Station since 1869 and so many of the old hands and early day doings. <i>(1952,My Yesteryears,9</i>) [NOTES: [f. c1869]]



## early bird
 
 <i>n.</i> a scab

>  <b>EVANS</b> There were many that would not mix with the 'early birds' as the scabs were sometimes called. <i>(1999,Where Giants Dwell:,38</i>)



## Early Childhood Centre
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  St Barnabas reverend Monty Black says it is not the church's intention to force the creche to close, but that the church needs the building back, to open a full-time Early Childhood Centre next year. <i>(May 29, 2002,Norwester,8</i>)



## Early Childhood Council
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [ECC](../E#ecc))

>  The Early Childhood Council (ECC) published a report, "Putting Children First", where it made clear that this decision threatened the very existence of private, for-profit settings. <i>(Oct 29, 2005,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7</i>)



## early closing
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an early shutting up of a paddock for hay.

>  Very early closing means a higher proportion of grass to clover, whereas late closing means the reverse .. By shutting up early the farmer is enabled to get the hay crop cut early ... [shutting up] <i>(Aug 20, 1924,NZ Journal of Agriculture,126</i>)



## early country
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land on which spring growth is early and fast.

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Climate - is your country early or late? <i>(1940,Te Waimate,96</i>)



## early cup
 
 <i>n.</i> early morning tea

>  <b>LUST</b> Felt inclined to have an extra hour's rest in bed, but the recollection that the servant might soon be bringing the early cup of tea made me hurry up - ... The `early cup' came as I had expected at half past six. <i>(Feb 22, 1875,Thames Journals,159,1975 ed.</i>)



## early lamber
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a ewe that lambs early in a season.

>  <b>STEVENS</b> .. as their time approaches and the early lambers have become reduced to a small mob, the two lots can be boxed and thr routine continued. <i>(1958,Sheep.  Part 1, Sheep husbandry,49</i>)

>  This country still grows appreciable amounts of grass in winter .. and this is put to good use by the early lambers. <i>(Jan 1977,NZ Journal of Agriculture,30</i>)



## early morning tea
 
 <i>n.</i> a former tradition in country life, or in hotels, of waking a person to a cup of tea.

>  <b>GUTHRIE</b> She was always cross when wakened early, even if it was for her early [109] morning cup of tea. <i>(1935,Little Country, The,108</i>)

>  <b>BOOTH</b> He was under the shower [at the hotel] when the early-morning tea came in ... It was the old stuff, tepid with soggy biscuits. <i>(1961,Long Night,104</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> The dairy farmer begins the day with early morning tea about three hours before breakfast. He gets the cows in, or musters, for morning milking. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,149</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> Setnets were strangers, and the joys of very early morning tea in a tin shack on a river-side also unknown. <i>(1986,Te Kaihau,38</i>) [NOTES: King Bait]



## early opener
 
 <i>n.</i> a hotel which opens its bars earlier than the usual time of between 9.00 and 11.00 a.m.

>  If they [sc. vagrant alcoholics] have a dog tied up somewhere, that means they owe someone in the place money. When they go to bed fully dressed that's `marching orders' and their `dawn parade' will be held at an early opener <i>(Feb 14, 1988,Dominion Sunday Times,19</i>)



## early settler
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The country at some distance from Port Nicholson is perhaps as good as in the neighbourhood of Auckland, but it is covered with trees of such enormous size as to make the expense of clearing not only a great drawback, but almost a difficulty scarcely to be surmounted by an early settler. <i>(Apr 22, 1843,Daily Southern Cross,4</i>)

>  It is not in vanity that we dwell on these passing evidences of the social progress of our settlement, and the growth of the forms and fashions of civilization, - it is rather that these notices may tell our friends in England - friends who are watching our steps with anxious hope, that the trials and difficulties of the early settler's life, have not diminished our taste or capacity for enjoyment, nor wholly deprived us of means of obtaining it. <i>(Sep 13, 1851,Lyttelton Times,5</i>)

>  Every early settler in New Zealand has had much greater difficulties to encounter than those which beset new arrivals, and the success which has attended the pioneers of the colony should be a sufficient inducement to the Nonconformist party to persevere. <i>(Dec 2, 1862,Colonist (Nelson),3</i>)

>  If to-morrow the whole country were thrown open to the 'free selector,' his privilege would comparatively be less extensive than that of the early settler who could take his pick of the 'Otago Block,' or his successor to whom the best land of all the coast country was thrown open. <i>(Dec 3, 1870,Otago Witness,1</i>)

>  Sir F. Dillon Bell. .. An early settler himself, he has shown the people at the other end of the world what sort of men they were who fashioned New Zealand out of the wilderness by the power of their hands and brains. <i>(Sep 30, 1891,Star, The,2</i>)

>  One of the pioneer settlers of Taranaki, Mr Thomas Oxenham, died at New Plymouth last week. He came to Taranaki from the South of England in 1841, and went through all the vicissitudes of an early settler's life, taking an active part in quelling the Maori rebellion. <i>(Jan 31, 1896,Colonist (Nelson),3</i>)



## early shearing
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the shearing of sheep in which fleecewool yolk has not risen. (See [sticky shearing](../S#sticky-shearing))

>  <b>BOWEN</b> An experienced shearer always breaks in new combs on the better shearing and when they are half worn down or ground out to the tips, they are put away for the early or sticky shearing. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,75</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> On lamb shearing, crutching, early or sticky shearing, no lead is put on, as it is essential that this type of wool be cut as soon as the comb enters the wool. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,76</i>)



## early shower
 
 <i>n.</i> Often in collocations, take, be sent for an early shower. a. In mainly team sports' (esp. rugby), to be sent off the field of play as a penalty.

>  Taking an early shower: Getting told by the ref to clear off and not come back for doing something beyond the pale. <i>(Oct 2, 1999,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  PETONE'S Pam Pickering was ordered off against Porirua ... Petone beat Porirua 2-0 ... but lost Pickering to an early shower after she apparently kicked an opponent. <i>(Jul 14, 1978,Dominion,16/8</i>)

>  Koteka's indiscriminate booting of his opposite Scott Crichton deserved the early shower but the punishment did not fit the crime. <i>(Oct 2, 1981,Dominion,8</i>)

>  In [referee] McCallum's book, anything that endangers player safety means an early shower. He won't waste breath on warnings for high tackles or spear tackles. <i>(Jul 10, 1988,Sunday Star (Auckland),C1</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> early shower what you can take after the ref has ordered you off the field of play for illegal activity. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,42</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a premature departure or dismissal

>  [headline] VARNHAM TAKES EARLY SHOWER A SECOND press officer has made an abrupt departure from Parliament. Mary Varnham, press secretary to Women's Affairs Minister Ann Hercus, left Tuesday in a surprise move. <i>(Aug 18, 1985,NZ Times,1</i>)



## earmarker
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an instrument used to cut a mark into the ears of lambs and calves.

>  We make the only earmarkers in New Zealand with tempered tool steel cutters that will never wear out. <i>(Apr 2, 1956,NZ Dairy Exporter,39</i>)

>  <b>DAVIS</b> Uncle Walter held the earmarker carefully against the pink edge of the lamb's ear .. Dropping the earmarkers into a tin of milky disinfectant, he pulled the lamb's short, fat tail out ... <i>(1962,Mutton on the Menu,155</i>)



## earn
 
 <i>n.</i> deal, sale, profit

>  ...Mark probably told you I have been swishing some luckies for him. ...`Swishing' meant to distribute or to sell. `Luckies' meant lysergide or DMA. The witness said he told Williams he just wanted to make a decent earn on them. <i>(Feb 16, 1982,Dominion,10/2</i>)



## ears laid back
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>SYDER & HODGETTS</b> Using the full extent of speed or power. .in 4;`Oh,yes, we had our ears (laid) back. We knew we'd have to work like blazes to get it all done in the time. We didn't stop to straighten our backs all day.' <i>(1962,Australian & NZ English [TS],488</i>)



## eartag
 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to place eartags on animals for identification purposes.

>  .. speeding up the road and going past our gate before we've had time to eartag a mob of 13 ... <i>(Oct 1999,NZ Dairy Exporter,96</i>)

>  It took all morning to eartag 20 calves. <i>(Apr 24, 1980,NZ Farmer,98</i>)

>  Ear tag on arrival. [ear tag; ear-tag] <i>(Jul 2001,Lifestyle Farmer,42</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> [Transf. f. farming use of distinguishing beasts by attaching tags to their ears.]

>  <b>HILL</b> Each year, the [dollar]1 donation ... has been eartagged for some worthy community cause. <i>(1988,More from Moaville,62</i>)



## earth oven
 
 <i>n.</i> hangi [Compare Maori oven, native oven]

>  From another very old chief I had heard of two having been once cooked in a Maori earth-oven ... <i>(1879,TrNZI,11/103fn</i>)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Food was generally cooked in an earth-oven. <i>(1904,Maori Race, The,109</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> After dinner he walked back to the back of the shack, where two little brown men were still fiddling with the earth-oven. `Looks like maggots to me,' he said ... `Hou-hou grub. Pretty good, eh?' ... `Pretty good, eh? No hou-hou, no 'possum, no wild pig, pretty soon we starve. ...' <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,147</i>)

>  <b>ZIMMERMAN</b> She showed me, too, how to make the little baskets called konae that are used to hold the food that is put into the earth ovens to be cooked by the heat of hot rocks, ... <i>(1946,Where the People Sing,65</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> (ed. note) Such hui were always accompanied by mass cooking, for the visitors, in the earth ovens or haangi. <i>(1973,Brown Man's Burden,134</i>)



## earth-crowder
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a horse-drawn implement to prepare head-ditches.

>  Earth-crowder. One of the most frequent and heaviest operations in the preparation of land for irrigation is the formation of head-ditches. This operation can be largely simplified by the use of what is termed an "earth-crowder." .. The crowder then comes into operation.. <i>(Aug 1924,NZ Journal of Agriculture,20</i>)



## earth-hungry
 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of settlers] keen to obtain land.

>  How does this earth-hungry would-be settler respond? .. He has not lost his appetite for land ... <i>(Oct 10, 1903,Hawera Star,2</i>)



## earth-star
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Each earth-star looks like a small round puffball when it is young, but later the outer skin.. of the ball splits open into a number of radiating arms, spreading out on the ground like the rays of a star, hence their name, Geaster. <i>(Dec 7, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,17</i>)



## earthflow
 
 <i>n.</i> [also used N Amer.]

>  ..a paddock was retired that had a deep-seated earthflow with cracks big enough to lose stock in. <i>(Feb 2005,Big Picture, The,3</i>)



## earthquake
 
 <i>n.</i> In various attrib., local, uses and collocations (see cits.): earthquake lake, earthquake mud (or clay)

>  Over the years different parts of the Karamea River have become blocked through earthquake slips. Locally known as earthquake lakes many are scattered throughout the area. They are quite distinctive with large numbers of dead trees littering the lakes. <i>(Jan 13, 1983,Nelson Evening Mail,4</i>)

>  One strange feature of the country [in northern Wairoa] is the presence of a layer of blue clay or mud in a liquid state at varying depths. ... This mud or clay is just like what the early Wellington settlers used to call earthquake [338] mud or clay, because it always ran out of cracks and holesin the ground at the time of an earthquake. <i>(1889,TrNZI,1/337</i>)



## Earthquake Commission
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  There will also be a chance to check out the Earthquake Commission's Quake Safe House. <i>(Apr 7, 2004,Capital Times,14</i>)



## earthquake weather
 
 <i>n.</i> Still, warm, humid oppressive weather, thought to forebode an earthquake. ContrastWanganella weather. [ca1942-3 St Patrick's Silverstream (Hutt Valley). The term was often used in these years of severe earthquakes in Hutt Valley and the Wairarapa.]

>  <b>HEBERLEY</b> Earlier in the day my mother-in-law had remarked that it was earthquake weather. The day was still... 'It's the eerie stillness.' <i>(1996,Weather Permitting,33</i>)

>  Even when summer was at its most brilliant, old people told each other that it was "earthquake weather". <i>(May 24, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),47</i>)

>  <b>WEEKES</b> The atmosphere was peculiar the evening before the earthquake, and an old Mauri `Huniko' told Lakeman at tea that we should have one, their name being Mumu for it. <i>(Sep 1841,Journal</i>) [NOTES: [cf. 1841 Sep Weekes Journal in 1940 Establishment of New Plymouth 55]

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> Weather report. Dead calm. Warm. Some sun. Earthquaky.] <i>(Nov 30, 1919,Letters,420,1951 Murry ed.</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> There was certainly nothing in it to suggest that this grey morning with it the blackest day their district [Murchison] had known, for the phrase `earthquake weather' did not then have currency in the district. <i>(1956,Green Kiwi,169,1960 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> The summers were hot then, earthquake weather my mother always said, and Tank's gang spent their desperate days ... rocking the swing bridge, hoiking goobs smack into the river. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,230</i>)

>  <b>DRYLAND</b> `Earthquake weather,' Aunty Mag said. <i>(1977,Curious Conscience,112</i>)

>  [Footrot Flats] It's very still ... and it's gone quiet ... It's what they used to call `earthquake weather' ... <i>(Sep 3, 1986,Evening Post,60</i>)



## earwagging
 
 <i>n.</i> [Is this a mishearing and confusion of senses of earwigging? + earbash and/or chinwag. Not recorded elsewhere: McGill (verb not noun below) prob. from Leland.]

>  <b>LELAND</b> Ear Wagging: a gentler (see:) ear bashing. Often used to refer to NAGGING, it can also refer to any RUNNING OFF AT THE MOUTH. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> earwag to harangue, but mildly, or to gush gossip; eg `While the girls are earwagging, fancy a game, old boy?' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,42</i>)



## earwig
 
 <i>n.</i> The native earwig <i>Anisolabis </i> sp.

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Native Earwig - mata, <i>Anisolabis littorea </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Mata[a], n. ... 3. Anisolabus littorea, earwig </i>; an insect. <i>(1971,Dictionary of the Maori Language,185</i>)



## ease off
 
 <i>v.</i> vomit [1981 Auckland Secondary Teachers College (Goldie Brown Collection) ease off: to be sick, to vomit]



## easer
 
 <i>n.</i> [(miners')]

>  Mr Taurima said the face measured about 30ft square. Seven shots were were laid - four lifters and three easers - and 150lb of gelignite was used. <i>(Feb 17, 1965,Evening Post,18</i>)

>  <b>McARA</b> The usual method of drilling was to start with the right-hand lifter, then the centre lifter, next the bottom right-hand cut hole, the knee hole, the stab (to break the outer part of the face and assist the cut) the bottom cut easer, then the hip hole, the right-hand cut easer, the two shoulder holes, the top right-hand cut hole, the top easer, the centre and right-hand back holes. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi,189</i>)



## easies
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  She said only tarts pierced their ears and she wore easies very day of her adult life, always of the same size even though she died two stone wider than when she first took a deep breath and dragged the first pair on. <i>(Apr 22, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),60</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> Later that year [1942], I bought some 'easies' (always in the plural), a tubular elastic affair that stretched from one's navel to one's thighs, with suspenders attached, and was designed to give more flexibility, as well as keeping one's contours firmly confined. <i>(1999,There Was a Time,208</i>)

>  Men were still on top, and many women were still in easies. Easies were tight elastic undergarments that gave a respectable woman a thinner silhouette, and armoured her against unwanted male advances. <i>(Sep 24, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> easies: GIRDLE; foundation garments. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)

>  easies women's elasticised foundation garment, called `step-ins' in Australia <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,42</i>)



## East Cape groundsel
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [groundsel](../G#groundsel))



## East Coast
 
 <i>n.</i> Usu. constr. with the, the eastern NI from Tikirau (Cape Runaway) to Te Toka-a-Taiau (the northern bank of the Turanganui River) at Gisborne. [Coast ca1988 Nov 10 p.c. Hori Ngata : East Coast - probably a translation of original Maori name for the coastal area Te Tairawhiti `the seasons of the sunrise'.] (See [Coast](../C#coast))

>  Aucklanders commonly call [the area between Poverty Bay and the Bay of Plenty] "The East Coast," but there is an East Coast all the way from Parengarenga to the Bluff. <i>(Jan 12, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,17</i>)

>  That while your petitioner makes mention of the progress of the natives on the east coast, he begs to observe he is only describing the condition of the inhabitants generally throughout the northern island. <i>(1841,GBPP,(No.311) 140</i>) [NOTES: 1840 Feb 1 (Poverty Bay) William Williams to the Queen]

>  <b>HOOKER</b> [Freycinetia Banksii, A. Cunn] Northern Island: as far south as the east coast. Banks and Solander [ibid 238] ... from the Bay of Islands to the east coast ... <i>(1864,Handbook NZ Flora,276</i>)

By 1867, manifold conflicting interests had grown up in Poverty Bay and east Coast districts, in the shape of land purchases ... <i>(1869,Dark Chapter from NZ History,10</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> And she would get me hoeing again and tell me about the East Coast up there under Hikurangi with the first sun, the corn ripening at Te Araroa and that lovely spreading pohutakawa between the cliff and the sea. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,43</i>)

>  <b>MASON</b> BRETT. In the last four hours, Sergeant, I've seen most of the East Coast! <i>(1969,Awatea,53,1978 ed.</i>)

It consists of the peninsula that forms the northeastern bulge of the North Island plus the area immediately inland from that bulge. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)

## eastern falcon
 
 <i>n.</i> an endangered indigenous bird of prey, the most common and largest of the three forms of the karearea.

>  Two of these [whitebait] species are classified as endangered, with a similar threat classification as eastern falcon, kereru and great spotted kiwi ... <i>(Aug 28, 2008,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  The Eastern Falcon inhabits the dry tussock land and rough farmland in the hills along the eastern side of the South Island from Marlborough to Southland ... <i>(2008,Internet - see Notes,2</i>) [NOTES: www.ranz.org.nz/raptor-information]

>  The Eastern Falcon uses no nesting material; instead eggs are laid either in a hollow patch of ground on a steep hillside, under rocks (see fig. 2), logs or on a rocky ledge. Eastern Falcons are sedentary birds with most established pairs occupying their territory year round. <i>(2007,Internet - see Notes,3</i>) [NOTES: www.canterburynature.org/species/lincoln_essays/nzfalcon.php]

>  <i>New Zealand Falcon Falco novaeseelandiae</i> GMELIN, 1788 ...[155] OTHER NAMES: <i>Karearea, sparrowhawk, bush hawk, quail hawk, eastern falcon, southern falcon, bush falcon. ... Bush falcon: In the North Island confined to the main ranges, ... Eastern falcon: The main ranges of the South Island ... Southern Falcon: Fiordland, the Auckland Islands, and Stewart Island. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,154</i>)

## eat
 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> smoke many quickly. [ca1956, 1986 still extant]

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> Lighting up the pipe he had been smoking. `Eating too many cigarettes, so took to the old gun, Bert.' <i>(1930,Bushman Burke,164</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>v.</i> to consume (a glass of beer) slowly; dawdle over (etc). [ca1949 Okaramio Hotel, Marlborough. He's eating the bloody stuff - get him a straw with the next glass.]



 
<b>4</b> <i>v.</i> eat out of tins/out of a frying pan, said of people who do not bother to cook a wholesome meal from laziness or ignorance; cf tin-opener wife [ca1940 at least We have been eating out of tins for a week while the wife's away.]



 
<b>6</b> <i>v.</i> eat somebody without salt (and pepper), to be able to overcome easily; to `gobble up'; to have for breakfast

>  Snap! - and very flat - goes a cap of a carelessly handled piece of one of our men! Talk aboutcannibals, I could have eaten that man without salt and pepper - a sudden silence amongst the Maoris announced their having heard the suspicious sound; ... <i>(1864,Von Tempsky Memoranda,19</i>)



## eat (one's) tutu
 
 <i>phr.</i> (Rural) [immigrants] to be dissatisfied or inexperienced with their new situation.

>  <b>PAUL</b> .. those who came out in the last two or three ships have .. passed with unprecedented rapidity through the crises of unreasonableness, false pride, and grumbling, which old settlers call 'eating their tutu.' <i>(1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,26</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> You will gather from this that I'm not 'broken in' And the troublesome process has yet to begin Which old settlers are wont to call 'eating your tutu.' <i>(1889,Colonial Couplets,20</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> .. as the saying is here, I shall have to 'eat my Tutu' and be very glad if it does not poison me ... <i>(1894,In the Land of the Tui,291</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> eat tutu, to: an early expression used of immigrants who were dissatisfied with their lot. After having been moribund for about a hundred years, the expression began to come into vogue again in the 1960s. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,29</i>)

>  In colonial phrase, they must "eat their tutu," and learn how to work whilst engaged in that operation. <i>(Jul 27, 1874,Timaru Herald,3</i>)



## eat (one's) tutu/toot
 
 <i>phr.</i> (See [tutu](../T#tutu))

>  <b>WILLIAMS & REEVES</b> You will gather from this I am not `broken in',/ And the troublesome process has yet to begin, / Which old settlers are wont to call `eating your tutu';/(This they always pronounce as if rhyming with boot)/ 'Tis that `experencia docet' they mean.[check L. spg] <i>(1889,Colonial Couplets,20</i>)

>  The Cockney new chum soon learned to `eat his toot', and he quickly acquired a good position in the district. <i>(1891,NZ Country Journal,XV/103</i>) [NOTES: (T.H. Potts) (Morris)]

>  <b>WILSON</b> I am only riding my hobby again, which is not the slightest use, for, as the saying is here, I shall have to `eat my Tutu', and be very glad if it does not poison me, for to utter a protest is to beat the air. <i>(1894,In the Land of the Tui,291</i>) [NOTES: 1892 Feb 9 in 1894 Wilson Land of the Tui 291]

>  Tutu,<i>to</i> eat. <i>See</i> under Eating t. <i>(Dec 30, 1933,Press, The,13</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <i>Eat Tutu:</i> An old expression in Canterbury (see<i> tutu</i>). Newly arrived immigrants took a certain time to settle down to New Zealand conditions. The following quotation explains the phrase: `Those who have come out in the last two or three ships have, I am told (with a few discreditable exceptions), passed with unprecedented rapidity through the crisis of unreasonableness, false pride, and grumbling, which old settlers call <i>eating their tutu</i>.' <i>(Oct 14, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BAKER</b> ...it was from this use that the colloquial eat one's toot developed. <i>(1941,NZ Slang,27</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Eat one's toot. `To eat toot</i> was the pioneer way of describing the period during which new immigrants settled down to the cold facts of New Zealand life. More correctly the expression was<i> to eat tutu</i>, for it was from the poisonous plant of that Maori name that the phrase was taken,' B., 1941: New Zealand coll. of ca. 1830-90. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,1040</i>)

>  Of the five cows landed from the ships, three have died, Mr Brittan's by falling over the cliff, Mr Fitzgerald's and Mr Phillips's by eating tutu.] <i>(Jan 11, 1851,Lyttelton Times</i>) [NOTES: 1851 Jan 11 Lyttelton Times in 1966 Turner English Language in Australia and NZ 165]

>  <b>PAUL</b> [The newly-arrived settlers] passed with unprecedented rapidity through the crisis of unreasonableness, false pride and grumbling, which old settlers call `eating their tutu'. [fn] `Toot' as it generally pronounced, the leaves of which may be eaten safely by cattle gradually accustomed to its use, is often fatal to newly-landed animals ... It was a very expressive saying, but had died out before my time. [1951 `before the turn of the century'] At least, I never heard it used. <i>(1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,26</i>)



## eat down

## eat out
 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to completely consume a pasture [often to stimulate regrowth as part of a rotational grazing or mob-stocking programme.]

>  <b>MACMILLAN</b> If you wish to eat out the paddock, then they can be left a day or two longer on each block. <i>(1980,Land Development in the Chatham Islands,17</i>)

## EBV
 
<b>1</b> <i>abbr.</i> (Rural) estimated breeding value, a measure of animal breeding criteria.

>  By selecting fine rams with high EBVs for fleece weight, Marty has been able to reduce the MFD of his hoggets .. By factoring in the Estimated Breeding Value (EBV) figures obtained within the flock ... <i>(2001,Wool Grower,Winter 26</i>)

>  These EBVs measure calving ease, growth rates, milking ability, fertility and carcass values such as weight, eye muscle, rump fat and marbling. For breeding, Mr Rowe first looks for an animal with a mid-range frame with good carcass quality, then looks at the EBVs. <i>(May 10, 2002,Dominion,15</i>)

>  The introduction of estimated breeding values (EBVs) was another useful tool in the cattle industry. <i>(Mar 31, 2002,Dominion,30</i>)

>  Some reference sires are used in successive years to create genetic links, which allow EBVs to be compared across years. [estimated breeding value] <i>(Sep 27, 2002,Dominion,C7</i>)

## echelon
 
 <i>n.</i> In the NZ Army, a contingent or specific part of an expeditionary force. OEDS2 1c. NZ (See) B 1941, 1947 C.V. Smith in Reid's Kiwi Laughs NZ; W3.] Used in WWII for a contingent of the Second New Zealand Expeditionary Force [French echelon[e1-acute] rung of a ladder]. [[Spec. use of echelon--> @a formation of troops'.]]

>  <b>WAITE</b> The troops were organised into three groups, labelled Echelon A, B, and C. Echelon A was composed of the portion first to land - <i>(1919,New Zealanders at Gallipoli,71</i>)

>  From an article First Echelon Reunion by Arthur Helm. The use of the word `echelon' in the sense of First, Second or Third Echelon has puzzled many people, for though it is a military term it does not normally denote a `contingent.' Echelon refers to the position of an army in the form of steps or parallel lines ... A new name was sought for Second World War soldiers, and `echelon' was the word chosen. It was first used in this context on August 29, 1939 ... While in the Middle East - in September 1940 - an attempt was made to alter it to `contingent,' but thiswas not successful, and `echelon' it remained. <i>(Sep 26, 1979,Evening Post,2/4</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  The following provision is being made here for reinforcements for the 2nd NZEF: Every echelon will include normal first reinforcements. The Second Echelon will include secondreinforcements for the First Echelon on a similar scale. The Third Echelon will include third reinforcements for the First Echelon and second reinforcements for the Second Echelon, also on a similar scale. <i>(Dec 6, 1939,Documents Relating to the Second World War,2/22,1951 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1939 Dec 6 Chief of General Staff to War Office (London)]

>  <b>BAKER</b> The New Zealand application, dating from 1939, of echelon to a division of an Expeditionary Force ... represents a new meaning applied to an old term. <i>(1941,NZ Slang,24</i>)

>  <b>JACKSON</b> The soldier of the 1st Echelon needed luck ... <i>(1943,Passage to Tobruk,12</i>)



## echo
 
 <i>n.</i> (children's game

>  Other throwing games which are mentioned today are: Echo which is widely known (Kapuka [19]29, Paerau [19]24, Stirling [19]35, Ngapara [19]29, Takaka [19]44, Mangatainoka). In this game there are two teams on opposite sides of a wall or shed. `A ball was thrown by one member of one side over the playshed to the otherside [sic]. The one catching it ran round theshed and attempted to hit one member of the otherside before a nearbye [sic] fence was reached. If anyone was hit, that person went to the otherside. The side that was largest when the bell rang, won.' <i>(1953,Unorganized Games,2/740</i>)

## ECNZ
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Electricity Corporation of New Zealand

>  Certainly, the government abolished the generating monopoly held by the Electricity Corporation (ECNZ). But, with the artificial creation of a "competitor".. the state-owned monopoly has become a state-owned duopoly. <i>(Oct 19, 1996,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23</i>)

>  Driven by the quest for market purity, successive governments of both stripes have one by one got rid of electricity forecasting, electricity planning, the Electricity Department, the Electricity Corporation (ECNZ) and - although we still have a Minister of Energy - the Energy Ministry itself... The ministryless Hodgson - who rather wistfully trefers to the "dear old days of ECNZ when there was integrated thinking" but quickly adds that "a ministry per se doesn't give you integrated thinking" - gets his advice instead from a department of the Ministry of Economic Development, an all-purpose bureaucratic pet-minder that services everyone from Jim Anderson to his dog. <i>(Apr 19, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),19</i>)

## eco-sourced
 
 <i>adj.</i> (Environment)

>  Over 600 eco-sourced native seedlings were planted in the first stage of this 10 or more year programme. <i>(Nov 2006,Footnotes,26 / 7</i>)



## eco-trooper
 
 <i>n.</i> an activist member of the Greens and eco-movement

>  Many of [the Green] eco-troopers are deeply sceptical about the trade-offs neeeded to govern, though the German Greens survived it. <i>(Sep 16, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)



## eco-whare
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a tourist display of a home and land in a natural environment. [ORIGIN: blend]

>  Te Puni Kokiri helped fund the development of Kaiwhenua Organics business and strategic plan, which includes the development of an eco-whare for tourists to visit the gardens and have a hangi, the development of walking tracks and expansion into manuka honey and free range eggs. <i>(Feb 2004,Kokiri Paetae,3</i>)



## eco-whinger
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The problem with eco-whingers is that they hardly ever tell you about the loveliness of nature. <i>(Jan 20, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94</i>)



## economic unit
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a farm property from which sufficient income is generated in relation to the investment of labour and costs.

>  .. this month's contribution to the series deals with economic unit increases incidental to the development programme .. the importance of economic unit increases from stage to stage ... <i>(Jun 20, 1938,NZ Journal of Agriculture,444</i>)

>  We have economic units for sale in all districts. <i>(Sep 28, 1961,NZ Farmer,69</i>)

>  Generally an applicant who has recently owned an economic unit, is not eligible for new purchase assistance. <i>(Feb 1966,NZ Journal of Agriculture,37</i>)

>  Asked whether, in view of the present situation, he considered a farm of 420 acres an economic unit, Mr Harty said .. 'They are sold as an economic unit at the time of settlement, but many of the settlers have substantially increased the carrying capacity far beyond our dreams' ... <i>(Apr 1968,NZ Journal of Agriculture,55</i>)

>  <b>FIRTH</b> The size of an economic unit has increased to such a degree that units that were economic with 60% debt in 1967 are now uneconomic even though they are now debt free. <i>(1988,Farmers are Bananas,149</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> When Keith and Margaret started out on their farm, it carried 900 ewes and 300 ewe hoggets, 55 hereford cows and a few weaner heifers, a typical economic unit at the time. <i>(1996,Trouble with Stock Agents, The,76</i>)

>  This has taken it from 3000 stock units to just over 5000 stock units, making it a good economic unit suitable for putting a manager on. <i>(May 25, 2001,Dominion,18</i>)

>  Counsel argued that the property was not an economic unit. It was unsuitable for a returned serviceman as a farmer, so the undue aggregation restriction did not apply. <i>(Jan 29, 1944,Evening Post,8</i>)



## economiser
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McARA</b> Economiser: A device using waste heat to pre-heat the water entering a steam-boiler thuseffecting a considerable saving. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),320</i>)



## ECW
 
 <i>abbr.</i> extreme cold weather gear

>  Just getting there [sc. the Antarctic] is an ordeal: an eight-hour flight on an RNZXAF Hercules, crushed on makeshift webbing seats with dozens of scientists and saupport personnel, all togged up like Michelin men in five layers of ECWs (extreme cold weather gear). <i>(Apr 2004,Listen Hear,No. 6/1</i>)



## eddoes
 
 <i>n.</i> In early NZ, the taro. [obs]

>  <b>FORSTER</b> A Comparative Table of the Various Languages in the Isles of the South-Sea ... [English] Arum esculentum or eddoes ... [New-Zeeland] Tallo [a-macron grave] <i>(1778,Observations Made During a Voyage,284</i>)

>  <b>CRAIK</b> In these spots were sweet potatoes, coccos, or eddas, which are well known and much esteemed both in the East and West Indies ... <i>(1830,New Zealanders, The,169</i>)



## edelweiss
 
 <i>n.</i> [Spec. use of eidelweiss, a European alpine plant with white, woolly, star-shaped flowers.

>  <b>PRINCE</b> The Saddle itself is ... studded with <i>Ranunculus Lyalli </i> (mountain lily), several forms of Celmesia (mountain daisy), and with Edelweiss plants ... <i>(1889,Diary of a Trip,9</i>)

>  Our first excursion was through ... beech forest in which we noted ... the gorgeous crimson mistletoe, and up ... to a height of 4,100 feet to the first of the Edelweiss. <i>(1891,NZ Journal of Science,1/202</i>)

>  Because we have here in New Zealand on our mountains two closely-allied plants resembling the Swiss one, which are usually known as the new Zealand edelweiss - viz. <i>Gnaphalium (Helichrysum) colensoi </i>, and <i>Gnaphalium (Helichrysum) grandiceps </i>... <i>(1894,TrNZI,26/343</i>)

>  On both sides [of the Southern Alps] grows the sub-alpine vegetation in all its beauty. You may walk knee-deep in Mountain Lilies, you may pick Edelweiss [fn] by the handfull [sic]. [fn] ... the Edelweiss does not belong to the same genus as its European namesake. <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,203</i>) [NOTES: (L.Cockayne)]

>  [Caption plate vi] <i>Helichrysum leontopodium </i> (New Zealand edelweiss), an abundant plant above 4,000ft. <i>(1910,TrNZI,42 :</i>)

>  <b>MYERS</b> `We have the Edelweiss, just like Switzerland. It's a little, flannel-like flower, found on the steep crags of our Alps. And in the meadows [160] grow mountain lilies ... Here, also are mountain daisies called cotton plants. ...' <i>(1941,Valiant Love,159</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> In spite of their life they [musterers] are usually ignorant of natural history; the alpine plants all round them they seldom know by name; even the snowy eidelweiss [sic] they know, if at all, as `eaglewist.' <i>(1951,High Country,44</i>)

>  <i>edelweiss n -es </i> 2 : a New Zealand herb (<i>Leucogenes leontopodium </i>) related to and closely resembling the European edelweiss <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,722</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Helichrysum bellidioides.</i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps. </i> [1885 MHG, 1917 DDD (Andersen, 7 entries)]

>  <b>GREEN</b> Here, for the first time, we found the New Zealand edelweiss (<i>Gnaphalium grandiceps </i>), and my men seemed to take fresh heart ... when we had our hatbands adorned with the familiar little felt-like flowers. <i>(1883,High Alps of NZ,196</i>)

>  <b>MURPHY</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps. </i> <i>(1885,Handbook of NZ Gardening</i>) [NOTES: And]

>  <b>HETLEY</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps. </i> <i>(1888,Native Flowers of NZ</i>) [NOTES: And]

>  <i>Leucogenes grandiceps. </i> <i>(1894,TrNZI,26:</i>) [NOTES: And]

>  This assemblage of [mountain] plants belong the New Zealand `Edelweiss' (no mean representative of the Swiss plant [190] from which it derives its name). [fn <i>Helichrysum grandiceps.</i>] <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,188, 190</i>)

>  <b>LAING AND BLACKWELL</b> <i>[Leucigenes] grandiceps (The New Zealand Edelweiss)</i>. [A tufted herb. Stems 1 in.- 7 in. high. Leaves ... silvery on both surfaces.] <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,432 (465),1940 ed.</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps. </i> <i>(1909,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: Report on a Botanical Survey of Stewart Island (And)]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> Finally, the New Zealand edelweiss must be mentioned, of which there are two species, <i>Helichrysum grandiceps</i> and <i>H. Leontopodium. </i> <i>(1910,NZ Plants,100</i>)

>  <b>DUNCAN & DAVIES</b> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium </i> <i>(1917,Descriptive Catalogue</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium </i> <i>(1898,TrNZI,30</i>) [NOTES: And]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium </i> <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story,2nd</i>) [NOTES: And]

>  <b>COWAN</b> White violets and pimpernels are plentiful [in the Southern Alps], and on the slopes above is, too, the New Zealand edelweiss. <i>(1926,Travel in NZ,Vol II South Island 70</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> `Common throughout the Southern Alps ...' <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,467</i>)

>  <b>HUNT</b> Included among the daisies is the beautiful New Zealand Edelweiss (<i>Leucogenes), ... </i> <i>(1951,Confessions,137</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium</i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium</i> <i>(1910,NZ Plants & Their Story</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium</i> <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story,2nd ed.</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes Leontopodium</i> <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <i>North Island Edelweiss, Leucogenes leontopodium</i>. Small prostrate plant with upward-curving stems the tips of which pass into thick woolly flower-stalks bearing beautiful flower heads ... It is found on subalpine and alpine herbfields ... <i>(1971,New Zealand's Heritage,1/266</i>)

>  <b>MARK & ADAMS</b> <i>Leucogenes leontopodium</i> (Hook. f.) Beauverd NORTH ISLAND <i>(1973,NZ Alpine Plants,160,179 ed.</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps </i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps </i> <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <b>MARK& ADAMS</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps</i> (Hook. f.) Beauverd SOUTH ISLAND EDELWEISS <i>(1973,NZ Alpine Plants,160,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MOORE & IRWIN</b> <i>LEUCOGENES GRANDICEPS, South Island edelweiss</i>. ... [The name edelweiss] is adopted for two New Zealand plants, <i>Leucogenes grandiceps</i> in the South Island and the larger-leaved <i>Leucogenes leontopodium</i> of the North Island ... <i>(1978,Oxford Book of NZ Plants,138</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Leucogenes grandiceps </i>SOUTH ISLAND EDELWEISS ... <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,202</i>)

>  South Island edelweiss, <i>Leucogenes grandiceps</i>, is not closely related to its Swiss namesake, but survives equally well in our antipodean mountain world.<i>Ibid.</i> 52 [Caption] South Island edelweiss, <i>Leucogenes grandiceps</i>, here occupying a cleft at Gillespie Pass, Mt Aspiring National Pass, is a member of the daisy family which seems to have a special affinity for bare rocky areas. <i>(Sep 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 53/36</i>)



## Eden of the Southern Sea
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>THOMSON</b> This `Eden of the Southern Sea', as it is was termed, like all other countries, had its defects ... It has its fierce `sou'-westers' ...; and in summer it has occasionally `nor'-westers', which parch up the vegetation for the time. <i>(1867,Twelve Years in Canterbury,7</i>)



## Eden Park syndrome
 
 <i>phr.</i> fear of being unable to urinate in a public lavatory

>  Local urologist Derek Rothwell calls their affliction the:"Eden Park syndrome" These chaps have a pee before they go out, apparently, and then won't go again till they're safe at home. <i>(Mar 22, 2001,Dominion,8</i>)



## Eden Parker
 
 <i>n.</i> (Sport)

>  ... it seems that that celebrated expression, the `Eden Parker', will soon be disappearing from the cricket vocabulary. The `Eden Parker' is, of course, the ball which scuttles through at about half-stump height, causing dismay to the quicker bowlers, disgust to the batsman wishing to play his shots off either foot ... <i>(Jan 5, 1962,NZ Herald,Sports sup. 3</i>)

## Edmonds
 
 <i>adj.</i> made from a recipe from Edmonds `Sure to Rise' cookbook.

>  Since 1955 every cover of the cookbook, and now even the new-fangled microwave edition, has carried a picture of the Edmonds factory, symbol of the baking powder empire, amid the gardens that have entertained Christchurch for almost seven decades. <i>(Nov 12, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  These two were not disciples of the Edmonds school of baking, preparers of wonderful sponges, scones or even hot dinners. <i>(Jan 19, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),43</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> She was the XOS ghost that came between me and the Edmonds sponges, the pork crackling on Saturday's roast ... <i>(1987,Swagger on Our Doorstep,56</i>)



## Edmonds Cookbook
 
 <i>n.</i> well-known and long-lived NZ cookery book

>  When I was in New Zealand I confessed I wasn't a very good scone-maker. I got back to Sydney and someone had sent me the Edmonds cookbook, with a cute note telling me to keep persevering. <i>(Oct 6, 2002,Sunday Star Times,D4</i>)

>  "We think they should come from the Edmonds Cookbook school of culinary expertise," said John Topp of Wheeler Campbell consulting. <i>(Jun 26, 2002,Dominion,1</i>)

>  The pages in the baking section of Fiona Farrell's <i>Edmonds Cookery Book</i> are encrusted with splatters from countless batches of date shortcake, afghans and chocolate eclairs... Other cookbooks might be bigger and glossier, but the <i>Edmonds Cookery Book</i> is the one found in the greatest number of New Zealand homes - about 72 per cent of them, in fact. <i>(Nov 4, 2004,Dominion Post,D1</i>)

>  In the twilight, ranks of label designs / rose like an Edmond's sunrise / from the dim-lit thirties. <i>(Jul 29, 1989,Listener (New Zealand Listener),8</i>)

>  You're no more likely to find recipes for their "Istanbul Steak" or "Fish Bosphorus" in a classical Turkish cookbook than you are to find "Wellington Steak": or "Fish Hutt River" in the Edmond's... <i>(Mar 7, 2002,Dominion,28</i>)

>  Perhaps the most satisfying story, though, is "Sure to Rise", a piece that evokes the passed world of mothers in pinnies - the Edmonds cookbook their breastplate against the world. <i>(Apr 7, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),61</i>)

>  A time to dig out the Edmond's cook book and brush up on home making skills. <i>(Feb 2001,Growing Today,66</i>)

>  In giant folders, the Edmonds Cookery Book ("why are they so damnably sure there will be anuprising, commissar?").. would sit alongside the rules of rugby. <i>(Sep 25, 1999,Dominion,18</i>)

>  Asked if he'd used one of my recipes, "No, Ma, just good old Edmonds". <i>(Dec 30, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

>  No household name foots it with Edmonds and its famous slogan, "Sure to Rise". The <i>Edmonds Cookbook</i>, the nation's biggest-selling book, has sold more than three million copies since 1907. <i>(Nov 12, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  <i>Footrot Flats</i> has been a pretty good thing for the <i>Edmonds Cookery Book</i> too. For a long time no decent article pondered on the amazing sales of the <i>FF</i> books without comparing them with the best-selling book of recipes. <i>(Dec 6, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),13</i>)

## eel
 
 <i>n.</i> Spec. use of <i>eel</i> for native salt- and fresh-water spp [goiro, tuna [dateline missing] 498 ...the eel is much larger than the English,...] (See [ngoiro](../N#ngoiro),[tuna](../T#tuna))

>  <b>McNAB</b> Q. What sort of fish did you obtain at New Zealand? ... A.There are snappers (schnapper), mullet, bream, soles, eels, and a great quantity of cockles, and some rock oysters. <i>(1908,Historical Records,1/550</i>) [NOTES: 1821 May Ensign McCrae's Evidence before Commissioner Bigge in 1908 McNab Historical Records]

>  <b>POLACK</b> fn Among the piscivorous tribes, are those well known in Europe under the appellations of snapper, tench, haddocks, pollock, salmon, gurnets, john dory, hakes, bream, sword-fish, elephant-fish, diodons, pipe-fish, cod, parrot-fish, flounders, skate, cat and dog-fish, sting-rays, eels. <i>(1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/202</i>)

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Anguilla aucklandii</i> Richardson. EEL; TUNA <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,17,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BEST</b> ..., thus disassociating the eel from all other species. <i>(1929,Fishing Methods,71</i>)

>  <i>Anguilla australis</i> Richardson. A specimen of this eel, labelled Chatham Islands, is to be found in the Canterbury Museum. <i>(1929,TrNZI,61/140 10</i>) [NOTES: Young]



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>HAMILTON</b> ...,with the following results: 581 eels (from 1ft. to 4ft. in length); 8 dozen flounders, of various sizes, large numbers of aua, or kataka (mullet); about 70lb. or 70lb. weight of pilchard, or mohimohi; a few snapper and kahawai; hundreds of red cod and rarii; and a number of kokopu or rawaru. <i>(1908,Fishing and Sea-Foods,64</i>)

>  [species name] eels, marine .. [scientific name] -- [other name(s)] unspecified - avoid using if possible. See CONGER, MORAY, UMBRELLA CONGER, SILVER CONGER, SWOLLENHEAD CONGER, HAIRY CONGER, SNUBNOSED EEL. <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>POWER</b> The swamps, ponds, and lakes abound with eels and lampreys of various forms and sizes, some of them hideously ugly and snake-like. <i>(1849,Sketches in NZ,78</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> The principal ones are the <i>tuna</i>, eel, the <i>pipiharau</i>, lamprey, the <i>kokopu</i>, and <i>inanga</i>;... <i>(1870,Te Ika a Maui,498</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND & HUTTON</b> There are two kinds of eels, the common one and the silver one. <i>(1902,Nature in NZ,76</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>MOTTLED MORAY</i> (Spotted moray, saw-toothed moray) <i>Gymnothorax prionodon</i> ... It is dark brown with a mottled arrangement of white spots close together on the head and more widely spaced towards the tail <i>(1982,Collins Guide,92,1984 ed.</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  A fish which the sailors called a silver eel. It was somewhat like a guard fish, it haf rows of brilliant blue the whole length of the body. [from accompanying drawing it resembles a garfish]. <i>(1856,Journal,IX/134</i>) [NOTES: 1856 Richard Taylor ? Journ IX/134]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  "It's a young eel, a yellow belly, probably about two years old." <i>(Nov 2002,NZ Geographic,No. 60/52</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (See [also silverbelly](../A#also-silverbelly))

>  [species name] silver belly eel .. [scientific name] -- [other name(s)] May be short-finned or long-finned eel ... <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  Whakaki's silver-bellied eels are the sweetest. <i>(May 10, 2004,Dominion Post,B4</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Ophisurus novae-zelandiae </i>Hector. SNAKE-EEL. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,18,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>SNAKE EEL Ophisurus serpens</i> ... The snake eel is the longest of the eels found in New Zealand ... However, the body is very elongate and even the largest specimens are usually less than 5cm in diameter. ... Long-line fishermen occasionally find these eels on their hooks and mistake them for sea snakes, hence the common name for this species. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,46,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] eel, snake 471, 482-4 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Snake eel Ophisurus serpens</i> ... Widespread in both hemispheres, subtropical. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,46</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> EEL, SNAKE (<i>Ophisurus serpens</i>) Long, skinny marine eel grows up to eight feet (2.03 metres) but rarely exceeds two inches (5cm) in diameter. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,65</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Snake Eel <i>Leptognathus novaezelandiae</i> (Hector) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,402</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>MOTTLED MORAY</i> (Spotted moray, saw-toothed moray) <i> Gymnothorax prionodon</i> ... It is dark brown with a mottled arrangement of white spots close together on the head and more widely spaced towards the tail <i>(1982,Collins Guide,92,1894 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> EEL, MORAY(<i>Gymnothroax serpens</i>) Several different types of Moray-eel occur in our northern coastal waters but only three are commonly seen. These are the Green Moray, Spotted Moray and Griffin's Moray. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,65</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> [THE MUD-FISH <i>Neochanna apoda</i>] is not uncommon in the spring time in running water in the horse-shoe bends of springs and rivulets which surround Masterton, and is there called `spring eel.' Waikaka is the name by which it is known to Maoris of the Wellington Province. In other localities it is called hauhau. <i>(1940,Fishes NZ,41</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Worm Eel<i> Scolecenchelys australis</i> (Macleay) ... 402 Worm Eel <i>Muraenichthys oliveri </i>Waite ... Worm Eel <i>Muraenichthys breviceps halituna</i> Whitley <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,402,1955 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> WORM-EEL<i> Muraenichthys breviceps </i>Gunther <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,139,2nd ed.</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> ?moray.

<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> An eel whose dorsal and ventral fins are the same length; one of two kinds of fresh-water eels in NZ. Compare long-finned eel.

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> There are two species of freshwater eels in New Zealand, one, the short-finned <i>Anguilla australis schmidtii</i> ... , having the dorsal fin about the same length as the ventral fin, and the other, <i>A. dieffenbachii </i>... , having the dorsal fin continuing well forward of the point where the ventral fin stops. The long-fin is usually predominant in cold water, ... <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,209</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>SHORT-FINNED EEL Anguilla australis</i> ... Unlike the long-finned eel which is only found in New Zealand, the short-finned species is also found in Australia, Tasmania ... <i>(1982,Collins Guide,90,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Short-finned Eel - hao, tuna heke (male), papakura (female), <i>Anguilla australis schmidtii</i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712</i>)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> <i>EEL, LONGFIN EEL, SHORTFIN</i> (a) Scientific name: <i>Anguilla dieffenbachi</i> and <i>Anguilla australis</i> respectively. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: (i) Longfin Eel - Eel (ii) Shortfin Eel - Silver belly; Silver eel. (c) Other common names: Freshwater eel. (d) Principal localities where taken: Auckland, Whakatane, Raglan, Lake Ellesmere, Riverton (Southland). <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,21</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Shortfinned eel Anguilla australis </i>... Alternatively, shortfin. Occurs throughout New Zealand also south-eastern Australia and some Pacific Islands. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,42</i>)

>  <i>Anguilla australis</i> Richardson. Short-finned eel. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/403</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Anguilla australis</i> Richardson. SHORT-FINNED EEL; TUNA HEKE. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,17,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SPEIGHT et al.</b> They are the long-finned (<i>Anguilla aucklandii</i>) and the short-finned (<i>A. australis</i>) the first of which frequents quick-running rivers, while the latter is found more often in lakes or sluggish streams. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,197</i>) [NOTES: Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Shortfinned Eel <i>Anguilla australis schmidti</i> Phillipps (Syn. <i>A. a. orientalis</i> Schmidt) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,401</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <i>Anguilla australis</i>: [European Name] Short-finned eel [Maori Name] Tuna. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,73 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> NEW ZEALAND EELS Long-finned Eel (Tuna): <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii </i> Gray, Short-finned Eel (Tuna Heke): <i>Anguilla australis schmidtii</i> Phillipps. [ibid. 125] The Short-finned Eel varies from dark brown to very light brown on the back, with silvery sides and belly, and is known as the Silvery-eel or Silver-belly. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,123,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLAGLAN</b> `So he went and caught a little silver-bellied eel, Doctor, just the kind she liked. ...' <i>(1965,Stethoscope & Saddlebags,112</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> They were silver bellied eels. <i>(1984,South Pacific Street,5</i>)

>  [species name] short-finned eel <i>Anguilla australis</i> .. [other name(s)] yellow eel, silver eel, silver belly eel <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  The short--finned eel (<i>Anguilla australis</i>) is found in New Zealand and south-east Australia, while the similar long-finned eel (<i>Anguilla dieffenbachia</i>) is found only in this country. [43] A good supply of wild eels, both the short-finned and the long-finned types, has long been taken for granted. <i>(Aug 2, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

>  Both our varieties of eel - the short finned and the long finned - are good to eat. <i>(Jan 24, 2004,Dominion Post,E7</i>)

## eel basket
 
 <i>n.</i> a hinaki (q.v.)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> [Otaki] my boys are feasting on a large tuna they caught as they came along. ... They have found a native eel basket which they have set, ... [Oct 3] ... they went to the hinaki and found it almost full they took about 2 doz. <i>(Oct 2, 1844,Journal,3/26</i>) [NOTES: Taylor Journal [TS:ATB]]

>  <b>BURNETT</b> Went in canoe with old Chief and Mackay to shoot ducks and set eel-baskets; ... <i>(Mar 22, 1860,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1860 March 22 Burnett Diary in 1948 Von Haast Julius Von Haast 88]

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Strong palisades of long posts were driven in, the walls inclined together so that eel baskets (<i>hinaki</i>) could intercept the fish passing. <i>Ibid.</i> 188 The eel-basket (<i>hinaki</i>) was nearly the same as that used in England for the same purpose ... <i>(1904,Maori Race, The,107</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The white man always tries to catch eels with a bob or hook, but when I was a boy I saw the Maoris get them in eel baskets ... <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,16</i>)

>  <b>BEAGLEHOLE</b> Trout are occasionally tickled in nearby streams and eels are more frequently caught with an eel basket. <i>(1946,Some Modern Maoris,98</i>)

>  <b>SHARPE</b> The only way to get eels out of deep water is with an eel basket, or hinaki. <i>(1962,Country Occasions,186</i>)

>  <b>MEAD</b> His boys found a hinaki (eel basket), which they set for the night, ... <i>(1966,Richard Taylor,64</i>)



## eel grass
 
 <i>n.</i> ? grass-wrack on the mudflats of the seashore

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Zostera nana </i> <i>(1909,AJHR,C-12</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Survey Stewart IslandAnd.]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Zostera nana </i> <i>(1909,AJHR,C-12</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Survey Stewart IslandAnd.1940 (Andersen)]

>  <i>grass, eel [s] Vallisneria spiralis L. </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,31</i>)

>  They are defenceless animals and depend for their survival on camouflage, being able to change colour to suit their surroundings, living amongst seaweeds, using their prehensile tails to anchor themselves to stems of seaweed. They live along the shoreline, generally among seaweeds or eel-grass down to depths of about 20 fathoms. <i>(Oct 21, 1972,Press, The,12/1</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Zostera novazelandica</i> SEAGRASS ... Eelgrass ... Green grass growth on intertidal flats, often forming extensive `sea meadows'. ... Stewart Island: Common, intertidal sandy mudflats. <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,320</i>)

>  We just picked up a nursery selling the aquatic weed eelgrass and yellow flag irises, which, while very pretty, are devastating wetlands in New Zealand... <i>(Oct 14, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)



## eel trap
 
 <i>n.</i> a hinaki (q.v.)

>  <b>TUCKETT</b> ... the track conducting into the Raupo became wetter and wetter, until it terminated at an eel-trap in deep-standing water. <i>(Apr 6, 1844,Diary</i>) [NOTES: Tuckett Diary in 1898 Hocken Contributions 205]

>  <b>BUICK</b> By January the eels were on the move ... to become victims to the cunningly made hinaki tuna, or eel-traps, set at the openings of the weirs ... <i>(1937,Moa-Hunters of NZ,175</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> It was a good life on the hydro. Men dragged home quarters of deer from the ranges to fill the cook-pots, ... or learned from the Maoris to make eel-traps, wickerwork structures which caught the big black-sided fellows down in the stream. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,126</i>)

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> When young we / bird nested / tree clad banks. / Set eel traps / in weirs <i>(1986,Countless Signs,100</i>) [NOTES: From Evelyn Patuawa-Nathan (1979) The Kaihu]



## eel trout
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  There are eel pouts (a species of trout) in some of the streams.] <i>(Jul 5, 1851,Lyttelton Times,7</i>) [NOTES: ?Is this confusing the name eel-pout--> with some kind of kokopu: cf (Torlesse)]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> The `Tikihemi' [Williams `a fish'] corresponds with our trout; in shape and appearance it is much like an eel. <i>Ibid.</i> 414 The settlers have named it the eel-trout. Takaruwha, a fresh-water fish a foot long, and thick in proportion, found in the Waingongoro; this is called the eel trout, and rises at the fly. <i>Ibid.</i> 499 ...the tikihemi corresponds with our trout; in shape and appearance it is much like an eel, hence it is named the eel-trout; ... <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,383</i>)

## eel-bob
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> [We] undertook to risk the reptile [sc. taniwha] if the Maoris would make us two `eel-bobs.' These are made by stringing worms on flax-fibre, which is attached in a loose lump by a cord to the end of a stick. <i>Ibid.</i> 44 The Maoris had our `bobs' ready, ... Presently an old chap named Paitai got very excited because a large eel was `stripping his bob.' The fibre was not strong enough to lift the large eel out of the water. <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,43,1935 ed.</i>)

## effluent station
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a depot for effluent from stock truck tanks.

>  Design of the facility will be similar to designs for the stock effluent station constructed at Allanton in the South Island. <i>(2001,Taranaki Effluent Disposal Strategy,41</i>)

>  A $140,000 stock effluent station being built ar Riverlands, 35 kilometres south of Picton, would be useless unless Wellington built its own facility at the ferry terminal, the council said. The use of effluent stations meant slippery stock waste on roads from truck spillage would be largely eliminated, making roads safer. <i>(Aug 11, 2001,Dominion,9</i>)



## EFTS
 
 <i>abbr.</i> equivalent full-time students [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  Those students translated to 2204 government-funded equivalent full-time students (EFTS), nearly 40 per cent more than at the same time last year... The university had enrolled 13,713 students by mid-March, equivalent to 13,675 EFTS. <i>(Mar 20, 2002,NZ Education Review,1</i>)

>  This was coupled with significant budget cuts, and a change in funding model so institutions were funded per equivalent fulltime student (EFTS) also known as "bums on seats". <i>(May 18, 2005,Dominion Post,B7</i>)

>  The college's annual report for 2001 said the college's business school attracted 313 government-subsidised equivalent full-time students (EFTS) last year, 46 EFTS in its bachelor of performing arts courses and 24 EFTS in its bachelor of sport coaching. <i>(May 29, 2002,NZ Education Review,2</i>)

## egg cap
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Pitching games were Pitch and Toss, Quoits, Ninepins, Egg Cap and Toodle-em-buck. ... There is still occasional mention of Egg Cap (Horoeka, Alexandra). <i>(1953,Unorganized Games,2/471</i>)

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Nevertheless, [pitching games] had their place [in the play of new Zealand children], and most important of all was undoubtedly Egg Cap (K-21), known also as Egg in Cap, Egg in the Nest, Egg Cup, Rotten Egg, and Mingle the Bonnets. It was a pitching, chasing, and ball-tagging game in which a ball was pitched into school caps or holes in the ground. Fortunately the game is still played at Alexandra, Central Otago, and has been recorded in some detail. [Holes or `nests' are 6 inches apart.] ... (Naseby, Otago, 1900; Nelson, 1875; Dunedin, 1885) <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,168</i>)

## egg floor
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) (formerly) a place where eggs were graded and packed under Egg Marketing Regulations; the licensed marketing agent appointed by the Poultry Board in all egg marketing areas, to receive, grade and distribute eggs to retailing outlets. [194? Blenheim and other towns]

>  Egg Floor: Licensed marketing agent appointed by the Poultry Board in all egg marketing areas, to receive, grade and distribute eggs to retailing outlets. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,47</i>)

>  egg floor NZ licensed agent appointed to receive, grade, and distribute eggs for sale <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,239</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  You'll be taking the eggs round to the egg-floor now, then, will you, Dad? <i>(May 14, 1947,NZ Observer,21</i>)

>  The collecting agency in each catchment area.. has been its Central Egg Floor. <i>(1945,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),2(23) : 17</i>)

>  The wholesale handling of eggs has been centralized in what are known as "egg floors". <i>(1945,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),3(1) : 30</i>)

>  The reason for the visit is to have a final discussion.. for the purpose of establishing an egg marketing floor in Timaru. <i>(Jul 7, 1943,Timaru Herald,4</i>)

>  One grocer I know is sure that the answer to the bad egg mystery can be found on the poultry farm instead of in the egg floor buildings. <i>(May 27, 1953,NZ Observer,10</i>)

## egg-in-the-hat

## egg-laying skink
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a skink unique in New Zealand for laying eggs rather than live young, and for its unusual habit of foraging under water. [<i>Oligosoma suteri</i> aka Suter's skink, and aka Diving skink] (See [Suter's skink](../S#suter's-skink),[Diving skink](../D#diving-skink))

>  One New Zealand skink (called the egg-laying skink) lays eggs, but all other species give birth to fully-formed young. <i>(2006,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.govt.nz/upload/documents/about-doc/concessions-and-permits/conservation-revealed/skinks-geckos-lowres.pdf]

>  The egg-laying skink is not only New Zealand's sole native oviparous lizard but also the only <i>Oligosoma</i> species that is primarily nocturnal. <i>(Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/71</i>)

>  Egg-Laying Skink <Oligosoma suteri</i> Found on islands off the Northeastern side of the North Island where it lives on rocky beaches even foraging in the inter tidal zone, mainly nocturnal but will sun bask. <i>(Aug 7, 2006,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://wwwreptilepark.co.nz/nzlizard.htm]

## eggbird
 
 <i>n.</i> (sooty) tern

>  <b>FORSTER</b> An <i>Eggbird</i> & the <i>Quackerbird</i>, both which I wish to examine. [ed. fn] <i>Pachyptila</i> spp. <i>(Dec 6, 1772,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/190</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We saw ... [in s. Indian Ocean] a single tern (<i>sterna</i>) or as the seamen call it an egg-bird, which had a forked tail. <i>(1773,Voyage Round the World,1/113</i>)

>  Sooty Tern <i>Sterna fuscata</i> (LINNAEUS, 1766) NATIVE OTHER NAMES: <i>Wideawake tern, whale bird, egg bird.</i> ... The sooty tern is the most oceanic of all terns; it ranges over most tropical and sub-tropical seas and comes to land only to breed. <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,233</i>)



## eggie
