## eh
 
<b>2</b> <i>exclam.</i> Modern uses of eh, usu. in Eng. contexts

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> The next day, Taupoki came to me and said: `Yesterday's degradation; you saw it, hey? ... ' <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,161</i>)

>  <b>OWEN</b> Hallo, Wiremu. How are you coming up? A hard case as usual, eh? [This is perhaps a transitional use] <i>(1909,Philip Loveluck,119</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> The fat brown man leers broadly ... `How you like, eh?' asks Fatty-face, swinging the fullkit forward for Uncle Ted to see. <i>(1948,Tidal Creek,40,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ROGERS</b> `You come to see the horse, eh?' Te Rangi said. <i>(1960,Long White Cloud, The,72</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> The Maori mother laughed, showing broken teeth. `She got a rash, eh?' <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,113</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> No. No thanks. They had a big parcel of fish and chips. ... There. In the dragon [a merry-go-round car so shaped] (like the taniwha, eh!). Poppy was minding it. <i>(1949,I Saw in My Dream,252,1974 ed. Rhodes ed.</i>)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> We were quite expecting our Maori shearers to lose another relative ...`So we stay and shear boss's sheep, eh?' <i>(1953,Breakfast at Six,180</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `I t'ink he pay,' exclaimed Mati [a Maori], with her usual little interrogative `Eh', meant to soften the positiveness of her assertions. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,203</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> He could speak beautiful English but often he put on the plurry Maori eh just to have a crack at us ... <i>Ibid.</i> 154 Just one of those Maori girls eh. Descended from the plurry cannibals eh. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,206</i>)

>  <b>PEARSON</b> I'm a full Maori and proud, too. ... Old Mum she meant I'm still a savage, that's what she meant. Just quietly, eh? Haka and tangi. <i>(1963,Coal Flat,43</i>)

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> And a boatgirl with tired eyes said to him, You got a moneys eh I saw you give that kid a moneys. <i>(1978,Glory and the Dream, The,210</i>)

>  <b>BROWNING</b> `Blay stuck-up bish, blay pakeha ... Her'n that other one, o' there. Blay town Maori things she's good, eh?' <i>(1978,Watch Your Language,28</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> Henare with an angry glare flashed back, `You clever bastard. You want the blood nose, eh?' <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,14</i>)

>  We nearly got one, Turei said. Ay Jimmy, ay Patsy, ay Reuben? <i>(1984,Some Other Country,172</i>) [NOTES: 1974 Grace `Earth and Sky' in 1984 Some Other Country]

>  <b>ROSIER-JONES</b> `So it's you. Not a bad looker. Take after your mother, e?' <i>(1985,Cast Two Shadows,63</i>) [NOTES: [?Maori nei spg e]]

>  <b>MITCALFE</b> `Samoa, eh? Another coconut, eh? We got to crack these coconuts, eh Gary, else there be no room for us kumaras, what you reckon?' So they were Maoris ... <i>(1985,Hey Hey Hey,16</i>)

>  <b>AWEKOTUKU</b> e: pronounced as in `ten'. Interrogative, or a way of finishing a comment. <i>(1989,Tahuri,101</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>exclam.</i> In NZ the general English interjectional interrogative particle often inviting assent (OED2 2. `Wasn't it lucky, eh?'), and possibly the interrogative use as a request for the repetition or explanation of something that has just been said (OED2 3 colloq. or vulgar `Eh? What's that, Sackville?), possibly reinforced in some areas by a use of the Maori interrogative n&emacr. produces a typical and frequent form: positive statement + particle eh? or eh! You're going to town, eh(?) J. Devanny Old Savage 1927 uses both the general English (negative) and the NZ (positive) use as part of her style (check pages 104 `aye'). Cf 1943 Bennett English in NZ 91 In the mild imperative with eh - as in `Give us a packet of cigarettes, eh' - it is tempting to see a reflexion of that egalitarianism that frequently strikes the visitor (who misses the respectful`sir'). [Used at end of sentence ca1951 Plute Warren `In NZ Services overseas the [positive] eh? was often taken to be an Auckland characteristic.' 1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) eh or eh? (at end of sentence): n'est-ce pas? -->1981 Auckland Secondary Teachers College (Goldie Brown Collection) eh]

>  I just jumped in the air and I remember thinking 'we've won, eh'. <i>(May 31, 1998,Sunday Star Times,Sport B1</i>)

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> `How long do you think it will take to get straight - couple of weeks - eh?' he chaffed. <i>(1920,Stories,230,1984 Alpers ed.</i>)

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> `We'll have some supper, eh?' [167]`Better take Glengarry, ay?' <i>(1926,Lenore Divine,141 (et. pass.)</i>) [NOTES: [spg also ay]]

>  <b>HYDE</b> `Money belt? I think the Turks got it, eh?' <i>(1936,Passport to Hell,120</i>)

>  `Which way did you come?' `Through Larissa, eh?' The final `eh?' spoken on a rising inflection, is a meaningless Kiwi-ism common to many North Islanders. It defies either explanation or translation. <i>(1944,Troop Target,29</i>)

>  By and large ... there is remarkably little difference in English here and in Britain. That is ... after making due allowance for such atrocities as `joker', `it's a fair cow', the ubiquitous `Eh'at the end of every other sentence, and the normal vowel flattening. <i>(Nov 1950,Here & Now,9</i>)

>  <b>CASEY</b> - chased him for three days up that valley, ey. 284 It used to be a real crackerjack, but I'dpuckerooed it completely, eh. <i>(1963,As Short a Spring,283</i>)

>  <b>JOSEPH</b> `I'm terribly sorry, Stan ....' `She's right, Mr Keogh,' said the boy with a grin. `We'll do it this time, eh?' `Too right we will.' <i>(1962,Pound of Saffron, A,152</i>)

>  <b>FRANCIS</b> `O.K. I've had that other dump, eh.' <i>(1964,Johnny Rapana,77</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `Can I speak to your husband?' ... `He's down at the back catching the bull, eh?' She spoke sullenly and her diction was slovenly. <i>(1973,NZ Jack,11</i>)

>  <b>MARSH</b> Bert stared at him. `That's a hairy one [sc. question],' he said. `What happened to the bottle, eh?' <i>(1980,Photo-Finish,173</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> Eh (pronounced as `ay' in `hay') Used as `DON'T YOU AGREE?' e.g. Hot today (over 50 degrees F), eh? Frequency of use decreases with years of schooling and increases as you go north, reaching a maximum in the Auckland area. It may come from the Maori word, ei. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)

>  <b>FRANCIS</b> There used to be an old character ... knocking over keas for the bonus, using decoy birds. He go ten bob a beak, eh? <i>(1983,Wildlife Ranger,79</i>)

>  SANDY: ... Anyway, cut it with the fucken trade secrets, eh? <i>(1984,Outside In</i>)

>  [Footrot Flats] Sorry Wal. I was a burk tryin' to be a big man for the sheilas, eh? I'll pay you back, okay? <i>(Sep 22, 1986,Evening Post,36</i>)

>  That's only 1 to 50,000, eh.... Let's get going, eh? <i>(Apr 1, 1987,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: National Radio programme [SchoolsSession: woman speaks]]

>  <b>GRAY</b> Mum said I can go away at Labour Weekend. Neat eh? <i>(1987,Stepping Out,61</i>)



## Ehoa

>  It is not at all unusual to see an Ehoa and a Yank, arm-in-arm. <i>(Aug 30, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,V/51 : 56</i>)



## ehoa
 
 <i>n.</i> Used in address `friend, mate' orig. and mainly among Maori, but extended to Eng. speakers as a term of familiar address chiefly among or between men, often to a person whose name is not known. Often joc. or contemptuously -->hee-haw.friend, mate [ORIGIN: Maori, 1953 14 M A7 Thames DHS 11 EHOA (EHAU =AIRHORE) 1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) eh hoa: hey friend] (See [e hoa ma](../E#e-hoa-ma))

>  <b>IHIMAERA & LONG</b> Come back and take J.B.'s place, e hoa. <i>(1982,Into the World of Light,215</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> A Comparative Table of the Various Languages in the Isles of the South-Sea ... [English] A Friend ... [New-Zeeland] Hoa [o-grave] <i>(1778,Observations Made During a Voyage,284</i>)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> [Maori woman speaks] Him plenty soak. Him better tomorrow, Ehoa. <i>(1936,Barbara of the Backblocks,18</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> `I'll be getting back. Haere ra, e hoa.' She drew the shawl over her black feathers and slipped away. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,170</i>)

>  <b>VAILE</b> [Central NI] Ehoa Literally `O friend': a common Maori term of address. <i>(1939,Pioneering the Pumice,289</i>)

>  <b>BENNETT</b> ehoa, an exclamation with the value of `look here', `I say', has become corrupted to the tag HEEHAW (as in `give us that fork, heehaw'). <i>(1943,English as it is spoken in NZ</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> `By cripes, he mean,' says Rua. `... He hold back my wages, e hoa.' <i>(1948,Tidal Creek,50,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> ehoa: (e hoa) a polite form of address equivalent to `O (or my) friend'. <i>(1949,I Saw in My Dream,273,1974 ed Rhodes ed.</i>)

>  <b>DUGGAN</b> `Sing a song, ehoa,' a Maori said to Harry. <i>(1981,Collected Stories,105,1955 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> (The Woman's Story) `One an one are three!' cried another [Maori child]. `You don' know your sums, ehoa.' <i>(1959,New Zealanders, The,33,1986 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> Worst of all [Pakehas] were the amorous ones who knew only one or two words: haeremai or e hoa, or obscenities; they seemed to think that the only way to ingratiate themselves with a Maori girl was to demonstrate their familiarity with Maori filth. <i>(1960,Maori Girl,219</i>)

>  <b>KEENE</b> ehoa: my friend, mate, general form of polite address. <i>(1963,O Te Raki,190</i>)

>  <b>BRATHWAITE</b> E HOA Friend. <i>(1967,Evil Day,379</i>)

>  e-hoa! n. oh, friend! Maori greeting. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),8</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> hoa: friend; e hoa, my friend <i>(1973,Brown Man's Burden,140</i>)

>  <b>HULME</b> She is obscurely hurt by that. `Bloody hell, Joe I'm not your son's keeper. ...' Easy, e hoa, easy. I was just joking sort of <i>Ibid.</i> 241 `I know you're close to them, e hoa, but I've never quite worked out who they are.' <i>Ibid.</i> 465 62 E hoa = friend, mate etc. <i>(1983,Bone People, The,116</i>)

>  e hoa NZ friend (esp. as term of address). [M] <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,240</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

>  <b>Ihimaera ed.</b> 'We all love you, e hoa, but your crazy antics...they're tearing this family apart.' <i>(2007,Get on the Waka,117</i>) [NOTES: (Kingi McKinnon)]

>  Dear Ehoa, - All the boys in this campthat have read the first issue of ourpaper think it is A1. <i>(Sep 15, 1916,Chronicles NZEF,I/2 : 34</i>)

>  Say! Ehoa, that reminds me. <i>(Mar 28, 1917,Chronicles NZEF,II/15 : 56</i>)

>  EHOA is the Vocative or form of addressand means O FRIEND. It is sufficient. Tosay "Dear O Friend" sounds queer. To cutthe O out before friend, you must dropthe E before Hoa, and then you would get"Dear Hoa," which sounds rotten. <i>(Apr 18, 1917,Chronicles NZEF,II/16 : 79</i>)



## ehouhounamu
 
 <i>n.</i> [Not in Williams: poss. a mishearing of ? e nanua pounamu.]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> EHOUHOUNAMU (<i>Chilodactylus spectabilis</i>). ... [19] Dr. Hector says it is common near the East Cape, and is called by the Natives ehouhounamu or nanua. The family to which this fish belongs - the <i>Cirrhitidae</i> - contains the hiwi-hiwi, the tarakihi, the moki, the trumpeter, and the porae, most of which are well-known fish, and stand, as it were, sponsors of its belonging to a good parentage. The same fish is found in Tasmania, where it is called the `carp,' ... <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,18</i>)

## eight hours/eight-hour day
 
 <i>n.</i> in reference to the duration of what was considered a fair working day within the context of the movement associated with Samuel Duncan Parnell, introduced into NZ c. 1840. Esp. eight-hour day, eight hours movement, eight hours rule. See also eight-hour man.

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> A hungry man will do anything, so [he] began his labours next morning at sunrise, for this was one of the few places in the Colony where the eight hours rule was ignored, where men worked ten hours daily ... <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,160</i>)

>  <b>FROUDE</b> Eight hours work, eight hours play,/ Eight hours rest, eight bob a day. <i>(1886,Oceana,209</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The Eight Hours movement has always been one of interest, and to Mr. Burns must be accorded the credit of its introduction to New Zealand. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,103</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> I was one of a little colony of men who had no respect for the eight-hour day, and to whom any semblance of government stroke was anathema. <i>(1913,Trade Hunting,[10]</i>)

>  Eight-Hour Day Pioneer Is Remembered A special ceremony to mark the Labour Day anniversary on Monday, October 28, is to be held by the Socialist Forum. <i>(Oct 19, 1963,Evening Post,21</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> [A meeting of carpenters in Wellington in October 1840 is said to have pledged itself] to maintain the eight-hour working day, and that anyone offending should be ducked into the harbour. [get complete cit.] <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,1/567</i>)

>  Labour Day was instituted in 1899 to celebrate the introduction of the eight-hour day. <i>(1988,Heinemann Dictionary of NZ Quotations,11</i>)

## elder
 
 <i>n.</i> A man [??person] of recognised authority in a Maori community, kaumatua.

>  We found him [Wiremu Kingi] & some of the elders together, but ... [sic] they all declined the discussion, ... <i>(Apr 13, 1860,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/562,1960 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (Rev. R Maunsell)]

>  [sc. Maori] Paul Temm feels essentially unchanged by his experience on the tribunal, sitting at marae around the country, listening to the elders vent their frustrations in Maori, hongi-ing with both tangata whenua and manuhiri (visitors), chatting over tea and kai. <i>(Jun 21, 1986,Evening Post</i>)

>  <b>IHIMAERA</b> To one side, a special group of elders was sitting, ready to give their speeches of welcome once the karanga of the women had been completed. <i>Ibid.</i> 74 There was a murmur of amusement from behind the matriarch where the ope ... were ranged in support of the matriarch and the elders. <i>Ibid..</i> 75 The child followed the pathway that her gaze had taken, to where a Maori elder, the first to stand to greet the ope, was preparing to begin his whaikorero. <i>(1986,Matriarch, The,28</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> tutu.

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> Nicholas-Narrative of a Voyage <i>(1817,Narrative of a Voyage</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> On emerging from Pamaki [a's-acute], we ascended another plain of some miles in circumference, covered with fern and bush, above which the tupakihi [a-acute], or native elder-berry, occasionally shewed its bending stem, yielding to the weight of the fruit, which hung in purple clusters. ... The natives, on perceiving me make use of this berry, warned me not to swallow any of the seeds, as the doing so would make me <i>ourangi</i>, drunk, or mad. <i>Ibid.</i> 1/140 The plain we passed over ... was covered with ferns (kaikatoa) [a2-acute : ?an error for <i>rohe</i>] and elderberry bushes. <i>Ibid.</i> 1/288 The <i>korai, koutu utu, miro</i>, and <i>putuhutu</i>, are wild forest fruits, prized only by the elder natives. The <i>tu pakihi</i> (Coriaria sarmentosa), or elder-berry, is a very pleasing fruit. <i>Ibid.</i> 2/134 The flexible tu pahihi (elder berry) with its pensile clusters of ripe fruit ... <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),1/110</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  Elder, wild . <i>Ibid.</i> 22 348 Wild elder slab. <i>(1890,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,9</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Leycesteria formosa</i>, =Himalaya honeysuckle.

>  <i>(1946,Bulletin of the Wgtn Botanical Soc.,14/8</i>) [NOTES: R.Mason, fide a teacher at secondary school-teachers' science refresher course. ?locality]



## Electoral Commission
 
 <i>n.</i> a statutory body whose principal functions include the registration of political parties, supervising their compliance with the Electoral Act and educating the public on electoral matters. [the info below appears incorrect: see Representation Commission (and its website)]

>  The boundaries of electoral districts are fixed by the Electoral Commission, formerly the Representation Commission (in Britain the Boundary Commission). <i>(Jan 1999,NZWords,2</i>)



## electoral roll
 
 <i>n.</i> a roll of the electors of an electoral district [Jim Cameron writes: The British equivalent is <i>electoral register</i>. For each district there is a <i>main roll</i>, required to be published each year, and one or more <i>supplementary rolls</i>. In addition, a <i>composite roll</i> may be published. There are no corresponding British terms. (TD: in fact ODE gives electoral roll as the UK term '(also electoral register)')]

>  We have electoral rolls where the British term is electoral register, although New Zealanders do register as electors. <i>(Jan 1999,NZWords,2</i>)



## electorate
 
 <i>n.</i> in NZ, the area represented by a member of parliament

>  electorate ...a) an area with a representative in a council or parliament. <i>(1979,Heinemann Dictionary of NZ Quotations,345</i>)

>  electorate ... 3. Austral. & N.Z. the area represented by a Member of Parliament. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,358</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1986 NZPOD; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 155]]

>  Dictionaries note the distinctive use of electorate in New Zealand (and Australia) to describe the area. legally called electoral district here, that corresponds to constituency in Britain. <i>(Jan 1999,NZWords,2</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

>  Besides less electric fence work, this has meant tighter udders. <i>(Sep 1959,NZ Dairy Exporter,35</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) an electrified or power fence [usually composed of light standards, treadins or pigtails, and energized wires. ] [in ODE with no label (TD)]

 <i>n.</i> marijuana [Frank Whelan (teacher) writes that he knew this phrase in Gisborne in 1965 and he dates this from going to University and finding it was not then to his knowledge used in Wellington]

So far as he knew, only one specimen had before been recorded in New Zealand - one described by Professor Hutton, and that did not belong to the actual genus torpedo. <i>(May 12, 1883,Otago Witness,12</i>)



## electric skate
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The hoki are sent down first and are by far the major species caught in this area designated G in New Zealand's exclusive economic zone. But there are also ling and school shark, headed and gutted before they are boxed, squid and silver dory, fat blue nose and silver warehou, hake and sharp-toothed southern kingfish, ugly green monkfish and slimy flathead, electric skates and others with saw toothed snouts. <i>(Aug 22, 1984,Marlborough Express,6</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> ELECTRIC SKATE: Another name for the Blind Torpedo Ray;<i> see Rays.</i> <i>Ibid.</i> 198 BLIND TORPEDO RAY A rare ray, <i>Typhlonarke aysoni</i>, as he is called in Latin, has the ability to emit sparks and make a needle magnetic - yet not to see. Variously named Numbfish, Crampfish and Electric Skate. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,61</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] skate, electric 315, 325 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> BLIND TORPEDO RAY OR NUMBFISH <i>Typhlonarke ayson</i> i (Hamilton) [ibid]. 92]. <i>This uncommon fish has several names, all of which relate to its manner of producing electric shocks, some of which are Numb-fish, Crampfish and Electric Skate. These vernacular names are given by fishermen on account of the fish benumbing or paralysing the person who unwittingly grasps one with his hands. The last name is from the somewhat similar shape to that of a skate or ray, ray being another common term for skates and also the scientific family name. </i> <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,90</i>)

>  An electric skate was caught in the Hauraki Gulf by the City Council's trawler, Cowan, and brought to Auckland on Monday. <i>(Aug 15, 1918,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  The second one caught in New Zealand of the fish called the electric skate (<i>Torpedo Fairchildi</i>), was captured in Napier yesterday. <i>(Apr 11, 1883,Wanganui Herald,2</i>)

## elephant
 
 <i>n.</i> sea-elephant; elephant seal. [also seal obs] (See [also seal](../A#also-seal))

>  <b>J COOK</b> We have killed all the Elephants that have come up here as yet. <i>(1852,Journal on Macquarie Island,1</i>)

>  <b>McNAB</b> She first made Macquarie Island and the captain [of the Venus, c1831] ... could see no signs of elephants. [=eleph. seals] <i>(1913,Old Whaling Days,88</i>)

>  <b>GRADY</b> The principal place where southern elephant seals, or sea elephants, have been slaughtered in large numbers near New Zealand has been Macquarie Island. The name `sea elephant' comes from the inflatable, trunk-like proboscis which is very conspicuous in bulls. Early sealers simply called them `elephants'. <i>(1986,Sealers and Whalers,53</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> shortened form of elephantfish

>  <b>MORELAND</b> ELEPHANTFISH [<i>Callorhynchus millii</i>] ... Other names are elephant and reperepe; the latter is Maori and seldom heard today. <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,12</i>)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> ELEPHANT FISH (a) Scientific name:<i> Callorhynchus millii</i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: White fillet (a trade name for the processed product). (c) Other common names: Silver trumpeter, Silver strip (smoked), Elephants. (d) Principal localities where taken:[Wellington and South Island]. <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,22</i>)

>  <b>BROWN</b> [=elephant fish] Not too many flat fish ... a good proportion of cod and elephants with a few rig sharks ... thrown in for good measure. <i>(1986,Weaver's Apprentice, The,91</i>)

>  [index to common names 283-293] elephant, elephant fish, elephant shark 380-95 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)



## elephant ear
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Alocasia macrorrhiza</i> introduced arum, from size and shape of leaf.

>  <b>ADAMS</b> Elephant ear is widely naturalised in northern districts. Its inconspicuous flower has a delicious fragrance,... The fruit head is crimson ...Botanical name: <i>Alocasia macrorrhiza</i>, tropical Asia. <i>(1980,Wild Flowers,10</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a kind of whitebait [(West Coast) (pl.)]

>  <b>McDOWALL</b> The pectoral fins are quite large, sticking out from behind the head, and facing downwards. This has provoked the name "elephant ears" amongst some whitebaiters. <i>(1984,NZ Whitebait Book, The,13</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> ELEPHANT EARS A species of whitebait. The young of the native Koaro, so named by West Coast whitebaiters because of their large and projecting pectoral fins. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,10</i>)

>  Here on the Coast, there is a kind of whitebait called `elephant ears'. It is fat and clear and larger than other varieties, but no one can give a definitive answer as to why it got stuck with the label `elephant ears'. <i>(Apr 3, 1983,Dominion,6</i>) [NOTES: Keri Hulme]

>  The young of koaro and short-jawed kokopu are milky in colour and are known as "elephant ears" because of their large fins. <i>(Nov 11, 2003,Dominion Post,A18</i>)

>  Tipping the goldenbait and elephant ears back into the water will help ensure the survival of our endangered native fish. <i>(Aug 26, 2006,Dominion Post,E7</i>)



## elephant fish
 
 <i>n.</i> a large, ocean fish with a long trunk-like proboscis, common in NZ waters.

>  <b>BANKS</b> We also that fish describ'd by Frezier in his voyage to Spanish South America by the name of Elefant, Pejegallo, or Poisson Coq, which tho coarse we made shift to Eat, several species of Skates or Sting rays ... [ed. fn] Elephant Fish <i>Callorhincus callorhyncus</i> (Linn) - <i>Creolians </i>call it Pezegallo, that is, Cock-fish. The French call it <i>Demoiselle</i> or <i>Elephant</i>.] <i>(Mar 1770,Journal,2/7,1962 ed.</i>)

>  [Queen Charlotte Sound] caught as many Elephant Fish and Mullet as serv'd both Ships crews. [fn 807] elephant fish <i>Callorhyncus milli.</i> <i>Ibid.</i> 3/807 We observ'd already that the principal fish caught by the seyne were Mullets and Elephant fish [ed. fn <i>Callorhyncus milli</i>.] & a few soles. <i>(Feb 12, 1777,Cook's Journals,3/797,1967 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1777 February 12 Anderson Journal in Cook's Journals (1967)]

>  Many other fish are equally numerous, answering to our <i>hakes, tench, bream, snapper, haddock, elephant-fish, pollock, salmon, gurnards, pipe-fish. </i> <i>(1838,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/322-323</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> [Maori form here not identified in Williams] Fam. CHIMAERIDAE 82. <i>Callorhynchus antarcticus</i>. - ... Inhabits Murderer's Bay, and other parts of the coast. It is the `erhe-perhepe' of the natives, and the `elephant-fish' of the English settlers. <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,2/226</i>) [NOTES: 1842 Gray's Fauna (Richardson's Fish) in 1843 Dieffenbach Travels in NZ]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Of the Fam. Chimaeridae, is that singular fish the <i>Callorhynchus antarcticus</i>, (Repe repe,) or elephant fish, which attains a size of fully two feet; this is found in Cook's Straits. <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,626</i>)

>  CALLORHYNCHUS ANTARCTICUS. Lacep. Elephant Fish. <i>(1872,Fishes,74 119</i>)

>  I have seen over a dozen sorts of fish in one haul, ranging from Barracoota and Elephant Fish down to Garfish and Herrings ... <i>(1877,TrNZI,9/489</i>) [NOTES: P. Thomson, Otago]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> ~ a fish New Zealand, South Australian, and Tasmanian waters, <i>callorhynchus antarcticus</i>, Lacep., family <i>Chimaeridae</i>. `It has a cartilaginous prominence of ths snout, ending in a cutaneous flap' (Gunth.), suggesting a comparison with an elephant's trunk. Called also King of the Herrings (q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,136</i>)

>  <b>BARRINGTON</b> <i>(1802,Voyage to New South Wales,388</i>)

>  In other parts king-fish in abundance, red cod, soles, and flounders were found, and large numbers of elephant-fish, gurnard, and schnapper, ... <i>(1901,TrNZI,33/574</i>)

>  <i>Callorhynchus anarcticus</i>, Lacepede. The elephant-fish; a migratory species, occasionallyoccurring in large numbers <i>(1906,TrNZI,38/552</i>) [NOTES: (Thomson, Otago)]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> reperepe ... 2. Callorhynchus anarcticus, elephant fish. [1957/336 C. milli] </i> <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,392</i>)

>  <i>The Elephant-fish (Callorhynchus milii) </i>is found in the New Zealand seas in enormous quantity. <i>(1918,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,1/136</i>) [NOTES: Thomson]

>  CALLORHYNCHUS MILII (Bory). <i>Elephant-fish; Reperepe</i>. In Christchurch large numbers are sold under the name of `silver trumpeter.' ... Distribution: New Zealand coasts (rarer in the north). <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/123</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  In general there are two main classes of the group Fishes, or Pisces - (1) the <i>Elasmobranchii</i> (which includes all sharks, rays, skates, and elephant fishes), ... <i>(1924,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/257</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  <b>SPEIGHT</b> The strikingly-patterned elephant fish (<i>Callorrhynchus milii</i>) with large flopping fins is well known (as `Silver Trumpeter') to all fishermen along the coast. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,193</i>) [NOTES: 1927 Archey `Native Fishes of Cantebury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> Bory. ELEPHANT-FISH; REPEREPE. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,11,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> Elephant fish. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,71</i>)

>  <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> Bory. Elephant-fish. Reperepe. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/401</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>POWELL</b> ELEPHANT FISH (<i>Callorrhynchus milii</i>) ... The elephant-fish is confined to the Antarctic Basin and the South Pacific ocean. The New Zealand species ... is common along the whole of the East Coast of the South Island and occurs at times to as far north as the Bay of Plenty. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,60</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Elephant Fish <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> Bory de St. Vincent (Syn. <i>C. dasycaudatus</i> Colenso and <i>C. tasmanius</i> Richardson) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,398</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> ELEPHANT-FISH (REPEREPE) <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> Bory de St. Vincent <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,84,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <i>Elephant fish</i> A sea-fish of Australia and New Zealand with a peculiar snout. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),139,8th ed.</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> ELEPHANTFISH ... <i>Other names: Callorhynchus millii; reperepe (Maori). </i> <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,194</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Elephant Fish - reperepe, <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> <i>Callorrhynchus milii</i>, the common elephant fish ... grows up to forty inches long. The long trunk-like proboscis is thought to be an organ of touch ... <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,201</i>)

## elongate dory
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>ELONGATE DORY Zenion leptolepis</i> ... Elongate dories are uncommon, being occasionally trawled in 400 to 600m of water around New Zealand, southern Australia and South Africa. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,184,1984 ed.</i>)



## Elytes
 
 <i>n.</i> new-chum diggers (phs `first diggers'). [(pl.) (obs. nickname)]

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> The uniform which covered all the scholars [in the school of manliness, independence, etc.] alike - `cook's son and duke's son' - as the poet sang in later years - was the blue shirt and the moleskin. The `Elytes,' as they were then [c1860s] called, went to work at first gingerly, travelling in state with pack horses and much mystery, and so much circumspection that they were repeatedly taken for brigands. <i>(1906,First Gold Discoveries in NZ, The,26</i>)



## EMA
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Employers and Manufacturers Association

>  The Employers and Manufacturers Association (EMA) belive the Bill will leave employers at risk. .. The EMA will seek to tighten the bill through the Select Comittee process. <i>(Apr 5, 2001,City Voice,4</i>)



## embushed
 
 <i>adj.</i> surrounded by bush.

>  <b>SLATTER</b> A land of glaciers bulking towards the bush, nikau palms bursting beside the sea, a land of embushed lakes mirroring the eternal snows and ferns garlanding the road to Lake Kaniere. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,76</i>)

>  The press is accused frequently of being too wordy. But we've got a defence, albeit one of comparison which came our way from Emfat... otherwise known as the Ministry of Foreign Affairs. <i>(Nov 30, 1996,Evening Post,56</i>)



## emigrant
 
 <i>n.</i> One who leaves another country (esp. Britain) to settle in New Zealand, esp. if assisted by subsidised passage money. See also assisted emigrant. [Compare and contrast colonist, settler; immigrant...]

>  I ... am to draw your serious attention there to as also the regulations to be observed on board the Ship by the labouring Emigrants. <i>(1940,Establishment of New Plymouth Settlement,3</i>) [NOTES: 1840 Nov 9 Letter of Instruction in 1940 Establishment of New Plymouth]

>  A school commenced among the emigrants' children - the cabin passengers kindly assisting the schoolmaster. <i>Ibid.</i> 190 Cabin passengers hold a consultation on the conduct of theemigrants [language; caning on Poop deck]; also as to starting school for the children. [check this citation] <i>(1940,Establishment of New Plymouth Settlement,18</i>) [NOTES: 1840 Dec 4 Weekes Journal in 1940 Establishment of New Plymouth]

>  [Describes difference between (a) Emigrant and (b) Settler - (b) is the pioneer of (a), and (a) has not the perils, responsibities or spadework of (b). Compare the prestige accorded to `First Settler' `Early settler' in the mid and late twentieth century and the disapproval of `immigrant.' Get full cit.] <i>(1841,Prospects of New Zealand, The,501</i>)

>  <b>35c</b> It is impossible, while there is ample room in the old settlements for all the emigrants who can desire to go to New Zealand, that Her Majesty's Government should consent to the indefinite multiplication of new settlements, ... <i>(Dec 22, 1849,Selected Documents</i>) [NOTES: 1849 Dec 22 Earl Grey to Governor Grey in Rutherford ed. Select Documents]

>  ... the condition of the poor emigrants whom I had induced to follow me. [1951 fn] The term `emigrant' was applied only to steerage passengers. The cabin passengers, mainly land-purchasers, were styled colonists. <i>(Sep 12, 1850,Journal of Edward Ward,19,1951 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ADAMS</b> They were immediately surrounded by a crowd of anxious emigrants, eager for authentic accounts of their future home. <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,17</i>)

>  <b>EARP</b> No greater fallacy can occupy the mind of an emigrant, than that by becoming a colonist he is about to acquire a sudden fortune. <i>(1853,New Zealand (various authors/titles),9</i>)

>  <b>FITTON</b> It is satisfactory also to know, that already ... a tide of immigration has set in so steadily to this [Wellington] province, that the excess of immigrants over emigrants ... amounted to 497 persons. <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),304</i>)

>  <b>MORGAN</b> Emigrants are arriving every week. ... What disappointment awaits them! It will be a hard case for some, I am afraid, especially for those with families. <i>(Aug 1863,Journal,68</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> The emigrants went to bed, and when ... I went up for a little time on the poop, I heard no sound save ... the rushing of the river as it gurgled against the ship's side. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,I/3</i>)

>  <b>JOSEPH MASTERS</b> In 1857, the Government employed a batch of emigrants to clear a cart track, through the three mile bush. <i>(1871,Autobiography,49</i>) [NOTES: (ATBMS)]

>  <b>CHOLMONDELEY</b> `Emigrant' and `Colonist' are used by some as simply convertible terms; though the life of every settler compared with itself, the condition of every colony, refute the mistake. Others, who are aware of the great difference that exists between the two, are yet very far from knowing wherein it consists. They sometimes speak of emigrants ... and colonists as two distinct classes, or rather, castes of men. One who flies from his native land driven by want ... who sets his life on a venture because it is worth so little ... these are Emigrants; while farmers, tradesmen, gentlefolks, those who have something, ... all, in short, who can make up a purse full enough to depart respectably, ... all who have prospects however distant; these are Colonists. I remember well on board the Charlotte Jane on the voyage out to New Zealand, certain friends of mine always called the common passengers - `the emigrants'. Evidently they considered themselves in all respects above the condition of `emigrants.' ... emigrant a term of reproach. [Cholmondeley says: `An emigrant is one who goes out without a scheme: a Colonist is part of a scheme'] <i>(1884,Ultima Thule,41</i>)

>  About twelve months afterwards [c. late 1850s] four English emigrants arrived, wearing the smock-frock then worn by farm-labourers. <i>(1894,TrNZI,26/564</i>)

>  `We never used to have these heavy frosts before the emigrants came in,' said the old Taranaki settlers some twenty-five years ago, as they thought of the golden days, when the `garden of NewZealand' was fenced off from the rest of the world by forty miles of standing bush. <i>(1911,TrNZI,43/439</i>)

>  <b>MACLEOD</b> At Lyttelton the emigrants were lodged at the Immigration Barracks until they could find other accommodation [in December 1863]. [NB change of point of view] <i>(1955,Voice on the Wind,13</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> On board the Company ships the cabin passengers, who paid their own fares, and who called themselves `colonists', while referring to the assisted passengers down below as `emigrants', amounted to twelve or fourteen per cent of the total passengers. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,94</i>)

>  <b>GRANT</b> [cf COLONIST] Events quickly caught up with most of the New Zealand Company's methodical planning, but the colonists (cabin) and the emigrants (steerage) were shipped to the new colony with model and thoughtful efficiency. <i>(1980,Unauthorised Version,12</i>)



 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>FITTON</b> Auckland is more likely to have attracted the attention of persons, who form their ideas of a whole country from the most salient points of interest ..., than the less populous, but, perhaps, more strictly emigrant settlements in other parts of New Zealand. <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),88</i>)

## emigration agent
 
 <i>n.</i>

Mr. Adam, who at the beginning of 1857 had been despatched as Emigration Agent, discharged his duties with remarkable energy and success. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,160</i>)

## Empire City
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> Wellington

>  Our Wellington contemporary [Evening Post] seems to have missed the point ... As the gentleman last named has gone to the Empire City on a visit, ... he may call and make this correction himself. <i>(Jan 18, 1868,Marlborough Express,3</i>)

>  <b>HINGSTON</b> [Wellington] has taken now to call itself `the Empire City', since its acquisition of Government business ... <i>(1879,Australian Abroad, The,363</i>)

>  <b>17/207</b> ) [The rat] descends into Wellington during hard weather, and if the Empire City is not spared, we must look for a like visitation from time to time. [check: this could be a refce to Auckland] <i>(1885,TrNZI</i>) [NOTES: Hector]

>  <b>SKEY</b> (`On the Electric Lighting of Wellington') To night [sic] from lofty shapes in trappings gay,/ The Empire City's bathed in mellow day;/ To night a thousand suns resplendent shine,/ From Lambton's curve to Newtown's far confine. <i>(1889,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,3</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> After the Bush, breezy Wellington looked somewhat tame, notwithstanding its smart name of `Empire City', bestowed on it by the newspapers, a designation which appears characteristic of London alone. <i>(1894,In the Land of the Tui,72</i>) [NOTES: 1890 Feb in 1894 Wilson Land of the Tui]

>  ... the boys from Wellington ... would have decided to walk back to the Empire City. <i>(Aug 12, 1905,NZ Truth,2</i>)

>  <i>(Dec 16, 1905,NZ Truth,26/7/5</i>)

>  <b>PINFOLD</b> For many months preceding the Prince of Wales's visit to the `Empire City', much interest had been taken in matters masonic. <i>(1930,Fifty Years In Maoriland,86</i>)

>  About 60 years ago four or five of us young men came down to see the sights of what we then called the Empire City. <i>(Oct 14, 1952,Evening Post,8</i>) [NOTES: [Letter from `Old Taranaki Pioneer']]

>  Amongst other traditions connected with the past history of the "Empire City" there is one respecting the existence at some distant period of a Chess Club. <i>(Feb 10, 1871,Erebus Papers,2</i>)

>  The Empire City, suprema a situ, and all the rest of it, has a fit of the most doleful dumps. <i>(Feb 8, 1890,NZ Observer,1</i>) [NOTES: Empire City is used for Wellington as opposed to Queen City for Auckland.]

>  They thought the Empire City [sc. Wellington] was about to be swallowed up for their iniquities. <i>(Aug 23, 1890,NZ Observer,9</i>)

>  [He] had brought under his notice the narrow escape of a Wellington resident from being buried alive during the recent epidemic in the Empire City. <i>(Jan 1, 1919,Wanganui Chronicle,4</i>) [NOTES: [Senior civil servant standing beside coffin with undertaker when noises heard from within; son then released, happy to have been saved from a fate worse than death].]

>  The Auckland reps. have proved their superiority over Wellington, and another victory has to be added to Auckland's already long list against the Empire City reps. <i>(Sep 6, 1913,NZ Observer,10</i>)

>  I was not long landed in the Empire City [sc. Wellington] when I became aware that there was another and bigger show to be seen than that at Parliament House. <i>(May 28, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,20</i>)

>  When first Wellington was called the Empire City, the appellation was used by Auckland journalists to ridicule a struggling town which was unworthy of taking the place of the northern capital as the seat of Government. <i>(Feb 14, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  It occurred in the Empire City,And had something to do with a strike. <i>(1920,Kai Tiaki,XIII/180</i>)

>  [Hdg] The Empire City. Progress of Wellington. <i>(Sep 21, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,22</i>)

>  He arrived in the Empire City at sixo'clock on Thursday night. <i>(Nov 10, 1883,Auckland Weekly News,14</i>)

[?=Auckland] <i>(1971,Speeches & Documents,106</i>) [NOTES: 1858 (1971) Speeches and Documents 106 quoting the Lyttelton Times Mr Secretary Stafford]

## Empire's Dairy Farm
 
<b>1</b> <i>phr.</i> (Rural) New Zealand [used mainly early C20.]

>  New Zealand has come to be regarded as the Empire's Dairy Farm. <i>(Jul 5, 1923,Hawera Star,2</i>)

>  New Zealand has been aptly termed the Empire's Dairy farm on account of possessing the climate and country so ideally suited for dairying. <i>(Aug 30, 1923,Hawera Star,10</i>)

>  Because of the practically ideal combination of a suitable climate and fertile soil, New Zealand's dairy industry, once supplemented by necessary modern production and transport equipment, has expanded till the definite title of 'The Empire's Dairy Farm' has been honourably won. <i>(Jul 25, 1925,Hawera Star,30</i>)

 <i>n.</i> (See [ECA](../E#eca))

## Employment Court
 
 <i>n.</i> a court set up by the Employment Contracts Act 1991 with jurisdiction over contracts of employment and disputes between employer and employee, including appeals from the decisions of <i>Employment Tribunals</i>. [Jim Cameron writes: A pale wraith of the former <i>Arbitration Court</i>.]



## empty
 
 <i>n.</i> In New Zealand esp. commercially and colloquially denoting an empty (liquor, esp. beer) bottle. [(often pl.)]

>  Get as many `empties' [= liquor bottles] as possible. <i>(Feb 24, 1914,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  <b>McCALLUM</b> [get cit.] <i>(1963,Half-Gallon Jar,41</i>)

>  <b>SYDER& HODGETTS</b> Empty beer bottles. .in 4;`We have to take the empties back on Saturday morning; my wife doesn't like a heap of beer bottles in the laundry.' <i>(1962,Australian & NZ English [TS],312</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a cow not bearing an embryo.

>  Grazing empties - culling rather than carrying over good quality empty cows is a traditional view ... <i>(Feb 1993,NZ Dairy Exporter,76</i>)

>  .. particularly in situations where the number of essential culls has been unseasonably high, the temptation to carry over a few of the younger, appealing empties can be strong. <i>(Apr 27, 2001,Fencepost.com,Expert Farmer</i>)

>  And what about those empties? There is a temptation to carry them through as hold-over cows, especially if they are only third or fourth calvers who normally perform well. <i>(May 2, 2002,Taranaki Daily News,18</i>)



## empty rate
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the number of cows in a herd which have not conceived or have aborted a calf in a particular season.

>  Mark said targets for this season include 950 kg MS/ha and a reduction in the empty rate to less than 5%. <i>(Oct 1999,NZ Dairy Exporter,84</i>)

>  Farmers who have used the product have reported an improved general health in their animals, reduced veterinary bills, a lower 'empty rate' amongst their cattle, and a reduced evidence of milk fever. <i>(Nov 18, 1999,Hawera Star,35</i>)

>  Nationwide, herds have been way off that magical score 5 by calving and it has had big implications on production and empty rates in the last five years. <i>(Jun 7, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,18</i>)

>  How many farmers complain of three-quarter cows, black pox damage, heifers that hold milk, high empty rates or high somatic cell counts? <i>(Oct 31, 2002,Taranaki Daily News,16</i>)



## EMTR
 
 <i>abbr.</i> effective marginal tax rate

>  EMTR is the effective marginal tax rate - your on-paper tax, plus what you lose from the government clawing back the benefit you formerly enjoyed. <i>(Jun 26, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),16</i>)



## emu
 
 <i>n.</i> [CHECK Cruise JOURNAL: also check Dieffenbach.] =moa]

>  <b>BRUNNER</b> emu (kivi: checkBrunner in Nan Taylor Early Travellers] =grey (SI) kiwi, also native emu, New Zealand emu. <i>(Oct 5, 1844,Nelson Examiner</i>)

>  <b>BEGG</b> Milford [139] Haven is a wild romantic looking place - ... the woods are abundantly supplied with game, as woodhens, green birds, emus &c - these birds are of large size, ... and as they cannot fly, they are ... <i>Ibid.</i> 222 There are [in West Southland] green parroquets, large hawks, and several kinds of wild ducks, besides Emus, green birds & woodhens, which are birds of an excellent flavour. <i>(1826,World of John Boultbee, The,138</i>) [NOTES: 1826 Boultbee Journal in 1979 Begg]

>  <b>104</b> The birds are not remarkably musical - the linnets are rather so; The Bell-Bird, is of a purple colour, with a white spot on the breast, shaped like a bell. The Saddle back, is a brown bird with a bright red spot on its back like a saddle, these two last species, are about the size of a thrush. There are green parroquets, large hawks, and several kinds of wild ducks, besides Emus, greenbirds and woodhens, which are birds of excellent flavour. <i>(1835,Boultbee Journal,1986</i>)

>  The kivi, called by the sealers the emu, is met with in great abundance on the west side. It is a common article of food with them, being caught with the assistance of dogs. It seems likely that there are two species of kivi, one much larger than the other. <i>(Jul 20, 1844,Nelson Examiner</i>) [NOTES: 1844 Monro Notes of a Journey (Nelson Examiner July 20 et seq.) in 1898 Hocken Contributions]

>  <b>HALL-JONES</b> [The editorial comment is wrong, it would seem, in giving the choice] Mr Bates also informs me, that a bird, called by the `old hands' the `Emu' (Apteryx), exists in the woods. It is about 18 inches high, and resembles the Australian bird of that name, [This may be a reference to either the small Forest-dwelling moa, Megal-apteryx didinus, or the large type of Kiwi (roa).] <i>(1971,Mr Surveyor Thomson,65</i>) [NOTES: 1850s in 1971 Hall-Jones Mr Surveyor Thomson 65 (Griffiths Collection)]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> A bird called by the `old hands' the `Emu' (apteryx) exists in the woods [on the south-west coast]. It is about eighteen inches high, and resembles the Australian bird of that name. <i>(1857,Southern Districts of NZ,318</i>)

>  <b>CAPLES</b> Near the mouth of the Middle Fiord [of Te Anau], they picked up a stone tomahawk and shot a `native emu'. The bird is described as being `curious among the feathered tribe, having neitherwings nor tail'. Caples goes on to say that `Other night-loving birds, such as the keewee and green-bird promenaded around our camp all night.' ... [104] However I favour the conclusion that their `native emu' was the large type of kiwi (roa). <i>(1863,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1863 P.Q.Caples Diary in 1968 Hall-Jones Early Fiordland 103-104]

## end of the golden weather
 
 <i>phr.</i> [Transf. use of the title of Bruce Mason's monologue celebrating the end of NewZealand childhood innocence.] [New Zealandism] (See [also golden weather](../A#also-golden-weather))

>  Climate turns to custard as the economy curdles. It sounds ridiculous, but the golden weather is going down with the dollar. <i>(Sep 30, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  [Tom Scott] End of the Golden Weather - Rainbow Warrior Affair [head title] <i>(Jul 12, 1986,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  With the clouds already dispersed, the sun has shone on Cullen's tenure with good weather, strong commodity prices and a low New Zealand dollar boosting export revenues. Now it looks like the end of the golden weather. <i>(May 4, 2003,Sunday Star Times,C3</i>)

>  Can you believe those end-of-the-golden-weather days we were blessed with on Thursday and yesterday? <i>(May 1, 1999,Dominion,23</i>)

>  The Automobile Association called yesterday's move "the end of the golden weather" after a summertime price war among the oil companies saw petrol go under $1 a litre on Boxing Day. <i>(Jan 20, 2001,Dominion,1</i>)

>  The first murder of a tourist in this country, it seemed to signal the real end of the golden weather - that long postwar period when people left doors unlocked and lone women hitchhikers trustingly accepted lifts from male strangers. <i>(Jan 15, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10</i>)

>  Wednesday's momentary end to the golden weather caused havoc on the roads with motorists failing to adjust to the conditions. <i>(Jan 31, 2006,Independent Herald (Wellington),1</i>)

>  That figure seems questionable, given that New Zealand has outperformed Australia economically in recent times but there is little doubt the golden weather is drawing to a close. <i>(Feb 1, 2006,Dominion Post,B4</i>) [NOTES: -]

>  [Caption] Trend of the golden weather You can't beat Wellington on a good day - the capital city basks in yet another glorious day of sunshine as the Indian summer continues. <i>(Mar 26, 2001,Dominion,16</i>)

>  New Zealand is nearing the "end of the golden weather", the National Bank's latest regional quarterly trends survey shows. <i>(May 27, 2003,Dominion Post,C3</i>)

>  Every New Zealand kid is supposed to have an End of the Golden Weather summer - a sun-soaked beach on which to idle away the days, learning about life and growing up. My reality was different. My reality was Karitane... There was a beach, but it was not a golden weather beach... While others have fond childhood memories of the golden weather, mine are more like the leaden overcast. <i>(Jan 1, 2004,Dominion Post,C1</i>)

>  You could try, for starters, the first oil shock of 1973. That saw the end of the economic golden weather and the onset of mass unemployment and chronic benefit dependency. <i>(Mar 22, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),22</i>)

>  For its own part, Labour's leadership would probably identify the 2002 campaign as the beginning of the end of the golden weather. <i>(Aug 1, 2003,Dominion Post,B4</i>)

>  Putting the clock back one hour at 3am tomorrow - the end of daylight saving time - may signal more than the end of the golden weather for some Kiwis. <i>(Mar 17, 2001,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  [Mason's] most popular work in the barren postwar period was the universally popular monologue <i>End of the Golden Weather</i>, about his Takapuna childhood. He played 1000 or so performances and it got through to all who saw and heard it. <i>(Apr 19, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28</i>)

>  No wonder Dr Bollard has taken to a bit of oral surgery, trying to warn house buyers.. that they risk a cashflow crunch from higher interest rates and falling prices when the golden weather ends. <i>(Sep 25, 2003,Dominion Post,C2</i>)

>  But in a lively, offbeat and totally unexpected scene set on a hilltop above Belfast, Cathy eventually begins to realise that the golden weather has ended. <i>(Aug 1, 2000,Evening Post,10</i>)

>  So let's keep on turning up to the Olympics, keep competing, doing the very best we can in the true spirit of the Games and then let's go home and have the best little Godzone life we can. Let's go for gold and the golden weather. <i>(Sep 30, 2000,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  <b>MASON</b> Interviewer: I believe the title was even quoted in Parliament a couple of years ago. Referring to our economic situation.. a Labour member noted that "Yes, Mr Speaker; this is the end of the golden weather, but <i>not</i> by Bruce Mason."Author: Flattering, but the title is the one thing in the piece which isn't original. It's from Thomas Wolfe, the American author of <i>The Web and the Rock</i> and <i>Look Homeward, Angel</i>, which I had devoured when young. <i>(1970,End of the Golden Weather, The,12</i>) [NOTES: 2nd edition]

>  <b>MASON</b> That night [sc. the night of the Queen St riots] marked an end; the end of the golden weather. <i>Ibid.</i> 95. I pick it up and somehow I know, as I finger the jaded petals, that summer is quite at an end. <i>(1970,End of the Golden Weather, The,49</i>) [NOTES: 2nd edition; 1st edition 1962]

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> The title of <i>The End of the Golden Weather</i> came from Thomas Wolfe's novel, <i>The Web and the Rock</i>, and its content is a gentle exploration of the secretive pleasures and pains of a twelve-year-old boy approaching puberty. <i>Ibid.</i> 39 Although the golden weather of boyhood is marred and effectively ended by the riots, its concomitants linger on into 'Christmas at Te Parenga', the season of ritual indulgence for children. <i>(1976,Bruce Mason,38</i>)

>  <b>DOWLING</b> That Christmas piece. What was it... The end of something. Something about summer. The end of the golden summer... <i>The End of the Golden Weather</i>. <i>(1982,Introducing Bruce Mason,16</i>) [NOTES: Bruce Mason refers to his own play in another play<i>Zero Inn</i> p.13]

>  Night two is an <i>End of the Golden Weather</i> occasion. <i>(Jan 16, 1993,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94</i>)

>  <i>Coaltown Blues</i> deliberately invites comparisons with Mason's works and attitudes. There is not much "in the way of golden weather" in this story of the life of a town seen from the perspective of a growing boy. <i>(Aug 18, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),39</i>)

>  [Hdg] End of the golden weather. Stuart Middleton looks back at smilier times. <i>(Nov 2, 2001,NZ Education Review,24</i>)

>  I liked the simple Jack-and-Jill prose, the American breeziness, the bit about "a lot of money". Already, though, I had come to the end of the golden weather in <i>The Sportsrwiter</i> by Richard Ford. <i>(Sep 7, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94</i>)

>  End of the Golden Weather : Why the great Kiwi Bach is an endangered species. <i>(Jan 19, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),1 (Cover)</i>)

>  The end of the golden weather - the phrase is endearingly familiar. Freighted with nostalgia, it evokes the days of seemingly endless childhood summers before the cares of adolescence and adulthood arose. Originally the title of a solo stage play, it has passed into our language, lodged there by the man who wrote the play and performed it all over the country for the best part of two decades - the great writer and actor Bruce Mason. <i>(Jun 10, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),38</i>)

>  [Hdg] Farmers' view of year points to end of the golden weather. <i>(Apr 13, 2002,Dominion Weekend Magazine,22</i>)

>  [He] also warns of an end to the golden weather, with the primary and export sectors already in for a rough ride and a projected slow down in the heated housing market expected to affect the economy next year. <i>(Mar 6, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23</i>)

## England's Last 'Ope
 
 <i>phr.</i> the Dinks

Rifle Brigade, The Dinkums, and The Dinks. Amateur army evolution? Sure! <i>(Jul 1, 1918,Quick March,26</i>)



## English
 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> Associated with Britain as a `home' country; originating in Britain.

>  <b>REEVES</b> And side by side with these strangers [`foreign' birds and animals from America, India, Germany etc.] and with trees and plants which the Colonists call specifically `English' - for the word `British' is almost unknown in the colony - the native flora is beginning to be cultivated in gardens and grounds. Neglected by the first generation, it is better appreciated by their children - themselves natives of the soil. <i>(1898,Long White Cloud, The,19</i>)

>  I must begin my English letter on the first opportunity, I have not any time to do anything. [71] 1862 Dec 21 Enys brought my English letters. <i>(Dec 4, 1862,Chudleigh Diary,69,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ROCHFORT</b> The town [of Christchurch] lay in a hollow ...; the streets were generally well laid out; and the snug little weather-boarded houses, (with chimneys made out of flour-barrels,) each standing in the midst of a neat little English garden, formed a delightful contrast to the wild hills around. <i>(1853,Adventures of a Surveyor,13</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>adj.</i> Combin. (?Collocations) English axe,?single-bitted as distinguished from an American axe. English biscuit, formerly German but with name-change due to WW1. English saddle =?? English sunbathing ironically for sitting fully clothed in the sun.

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> The Colonist's attention should be directed to the following articles, with which he must provide himself according to his means and his intentions. For clearing and cultivating timbered land:- American axes, of various sizes. ... Common English axes. (The natives can use these best). ... <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,447</i>)

>  ENGLISH BISCUITS Beat butter and sugar ... Add spices and salt. Beat eggs ... Add eggs, cornflour and ... flour ... to butter and sugar ... Add baking powder ... Roll out ... Cut in rounds ... Bake ... Place in pairs with jam between and icing on top ... sprinkle with coloured sugar ... <i>(1915,Our Boys Cookery Book,123</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> When my father reached the colony for which he had left England ... he bought a horse, and started up-country ... on one of the last days of November 1890. He had taken an English saddle with him, and a couple of roomy and strongly made saddle-bags. <i>(1901,Erewhon Revisited,ch. ii 207,Everyman edn (1960) ed.</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> English Sunbathing. The risky and risque exposure of one's ankles (possibly even the whole leg below the knee) to the rays of the sun while [37] ensuring the rest of one's body is swathed in thick layers of woollen clothing. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,36</i>)



 
<b>4</b> <i>n.</i> European; =Pakeha.

>  <b>SWAINSON</b> All owners to be free to dig Gold on their own land, without payment to Government, but not to permit other persons, whether Natives or English, to dig without a License. <i>(1853,Auckland,157. 4</i>) [NOTES: 1852 Nov 30 Agreement between the Government and Native Tribes for ... Gold Fields on the Thames ... in 1853 Swainson Auckland]

>  (Ngata) St. Stephens. This institution is now out at Bombay. I attended the opening ceremony, and hearing that the Trustees have decided to introduce a large number of English [ed fn: Pakeha boys, not English migrants] boys and to raise the fee to [pounds]60 a year had a go at the Trustees over the new policy. <i>(Oct 16, 1931,Na To Hoa Aroha,2/227,1987 ed.</i>)

## English grass
 
 <i>n.</i> usu. pl. [Contrast native grass]

>  [Hokianga] would be splendid sheep country; if English grasses could be got to grow upon it; there is not a single blade of grass indigenous to the country to be found on any part of the Island that I have yet visited; ... <i>(Aug 9, 1834,NZ Independent,33,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>YATE</b> The native grasses flourish throughout the year ... All English grasses flourish well; but the white clover never seeds; ... <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),75</i>)

>  <b>MACKIE</b> In his paddocks he has several of the english grasses, they are found to be very advantageous here, being hardier than the native grasses, they produce more herbage through the winter. <i>(May 2, 1853,Traveller under Concern,104,1973 ed.</i>)

>  <b>EARP</b> [cf GRASS, NATIVE] `A few words on the native grasses will not be out of place. `The grasses of the Wairarapa ... are very numerous, and if cultivated, would be fully equal to English grasses: ... There is abundance of native rye-grass, and the sheep are very fond of a species of catch-grass <i>(1853,New Zealand (various authors/titles),125</i>) [NOTES: 1853 Allom letter in 1853 Earp NZ]

>  The farmers of the old established settlements of the Northern Island, ... reckon March to be the best period of the year for sowing English Grasses and Clovers. <i>(1855,Wellington and Canterbury Almanack,66</i>)

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> ... 3rd, that the same wild lands, rudely sown with English grasses, would graze four sheep to one acre - ... <i>(1857,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/404</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> The all-engrossing topics [in Canterbury] seemed to be sheep, horses, dogs, cattle, English grasses, paddocks, bush and so forth. I was rather startled to hear one gentleman ask another whether he meant to wash this year, and receive the answer `No.' I soon discovered that a person's sheep are himself. He does not wash his sheep before shearing, but he washes; and, most marvellous of all, it is not his sheep which lamb, but he `lambs down' himself. <i> Ibid.</i> iv 43 Another mode of investment highly spoken of is that of buying land and laying it down in English grass, thus making a permanent estate of it. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,[Chapter III] 31</i>) [NOTES: (1860 Jan 27) 1863 Butler First Year]

>  <b>BUTLER</b> New Zealand grass is not a sward like English grass but it consists chiefly of large yellow tussocks of a very stiff, tough, hard grass ... <i>(1860,Forest Creek MS,48,1960 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BARKER</b> ... the ground immediately outside the house has been dug up, and is awaiting the spring to be sown with English grass; ... <i>(1866,Station Life in NZ,63,1870 ed.</i>)

>  The grasses of Otago ... might prove ... more nutritious than English grasses. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/22</i>) [NOTES: Buchanan Botany Otago]

>  ... [sic] you could not find a better man ... for Pareora as he understands agriculture and the feeding of English grass and crops.' <i>(1870,Letter</i>) [NOTES: c1870 Davidson to McLean in 1981 Crawford Station Years 59 `]

>  That section I bought after the three years expired and have that down in English grass. <i>(Sep 27, 1879,Letter</i>) [NOTES: [1879, Sep 27 Letter from Nelson in] -->1979 Crumpton Spencer: the Gold Seeker 182]

>  <b>KNOX</b> .. the lawns [of Christchurch] are velvety with English grasses, and along the banks of the winding river the weeping-willow droops almost to the surface of the water. <i>(1888,Boy Travellers,265</i>)

>  <b>SKEY</b> Ay, much he likes to see the grass,/ The English grass once more;/ To see a waving toothsome mass,/ Where stood the waste before. <i>(1889,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,144</i>)

>  A good deal of it has been burnt, and apparently surface-sown with English grasses. <i>(1913,TrNZI,45/278</i>)

>  <b>McDONALD</b> English grasses, including cocksfoot, and long-wool and cross-bred sheep go hand in hand [sc. ryegrass, fox-tail, poa pratensis, and prairie]. <i>Ibid.</i> 119 The fallen bush lands and some of the scrub lands have been sown in English grasses ... <i>(1920,Australian & NZ Sheep Farming,110</i>)

>  <b>PERRY</b> Nearly all the ordinary commercial flocks in New Zealand started from the Merino, but change of pasture from Native to English grasses ... has driven the Merino back to the high country. <i>(1922,Sheep Farming in NZ,50</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Of these station aliens, England had supplied par excellence `England grass', that is ryegrass, white clover, and cock's-foot; <i>(1926,Tutira,305</i>)

>  The grasses used were largely the best of the English grasses and clovers such as perennial rye grass, cocksfoot, dogstail and white clover which, incidentally, are not found to any marked extent in hill pastures in Great Britain. <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,281</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> Many an aspersion is cast at a danthonia knoll waving white with rank growth, and the grass is condemned as being unpalatable, whereas the person casting the aspersion is probably standing on the very same grass eaten down so well that it is to him `English grass'. ... the better English grasses are seldom seen to much advantage when growing within a danthonia or a browntop sward. I consider the stunted, ill-natured herbage of the better English grasses is less palatablethan the young, fresh herbage of the second rate species. <i>Ibid.</i>166 The early settlers of a century ago introduced to New Zealand various `English' grasses and clovers to supplement and later to replace the native grasses on the average-to-superior soil types. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,134,1970 ed.</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> The floor and gently sloping sides were verdant with English and native grasses from the broad strath at our feet right up to where the cleared country narrowed and ran out in the higher ground of the bush range at the head. <i>(1953,Golden Bush, The,41,1963 ed.</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> The clumps of tussock ... sheltered a scattered growth of English grasses, of dainty wild flowers ..., a delicate little blue-bell, almesia, gentian, and ... the bidibid which hooked itslef onto the sheep's wool to form great tangles. <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,37</i>)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> I'm dead keen on aerial topdressing. The results are there ... sheep go for this English grass. <i>(1982,Historic Sheep Stations NI,7</i>)

>  <b>RENNIE</b> ... sowing it with English grasses and clovers. <i>(1988,Super Man,297</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) non-indigenous pasture grass first brought from England [from the early nineteenth century.]

>  <b>DEANS</b> .. besides these we have a garden fenced of about two acres, and four acres around the house fenced and sown down with English grasses which do remarkably well ... <i>(Aug 3, 1848,Letter,51</i>) [NOTES: in Deans (1964) Pioneers on Port Cooper Plains]

>  <b>44</b> Another mode of investment highly spoken of is that of buying land and laying it down in English grass, thus making a permanent estate of it. <i>(1860,BLANK ENTRY</i>) [NOTES: in BUTLER First Year (1863)]

>  <b>PRATT</b> .. the land grubbed and prepared for laying down in English grass ... <i>(1877,Colonial Experiences,95</i>)

>  <b>MACDONALD</b> It is not wise to constantly graze a good pasture of artificial grass. Artificial pasture - that sown with English grasses. <i>(1920,Australian & NZ Sheep Farming,117</i>)

>  If English grasses are running out in different parts of the farm, try shutting up a paddock to be cleaned up and seed tramped in by cattle later on. <i>(Apr 20, 1922,NZ Journal of Agriculture,221</i>)

>  Pastures were slowly established by felling the bush in winter and spring, burning the fallen timber and underscrub in the summer, and surface sowing English grasses and clovers on the ash of the burns. <i>(Apr 16, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,346</i>)

>  <b>GASCOIGNE</b> .. when the vast fires were dead, the pioneer sowed his burn with English grasses which throve on the fertility of the ash-bed. <i>(1965,From the Top of a Hill,58</i>)

>  The property is all in English grasses and clovers. <i>(Sep 13, 1973,NZ Farmer,135</i>)

>  Apart from areas planted in shelter belts and a stand of Douglas Fir the property is all in English grasses. <i>(Dec 24, 1988,NZ Farmer,41</i>)

>  Most of the property is planted in English grasses, with the balance being natives and forest ... <i>(Mar 30, 2001,Dominion,22</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> - put three bullocks into English grass paddock. <i>(Oct 27, 1869,Journal Kept at Rockwood and the Point,199</i>)

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> On the English grass lawns of Lovel Hall and other handsome [Auckland] homes peacocks spread their dazzling tails <i>(1938,Promenade,175</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Except for Eyrewell, Glenmark was probably the only place in Canterbury where pastoralists tried to get English grass paddocks back into native pasture before sowing tussock seed. <i>(1975,Early Canterbury Runs,ch ix 278 278,4th ed. ed.</i>)



## English Johnny
 
 <i>n.</i> [cf. Tommy]

>  <b>MANDER</b> Come along and see the johnny. He's an Englishman. Going back Home. A queer duck. <i>(1925,Allen Adair,5,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> He had ridden over to the township with four of the boys to help hunt down the young fellowwho'd murdered Mr Williamson. ... Willie Cox said they found him in the barn, shot bang through the head, the young English `johnny' who'd been on the station learning farming - disappeared. <i>(1913,Stories,134,1984 Alpers ed.</i>) [NOTES: (Millie)]

>  <b>HEINZ</b> After the roaring days had passed away, there arrived at Hokitika a typical English `Johnnie'. He could distinguish gold from sawdust but had no knowledge of goldmining. <i>(1974,Bright Fine Gold,141</i>) [NOTES: 1874 `Hermul' in 1974 Heinz Bright Fine Gold]



## English mackerel
 
 <i>n.</i> = blue mackerel

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> COMMON MACKEREL ... <i>Other names: Scomber japonicus</i>; southern mackerel, English mackerel, frigate mackerel; tawatawa (Maori). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,267</i>)

>  <b>MIDDLETON</b> Don caught several English mackerel which were just beginning to come in ... <i>(1959,Stone, The,13</i>) [NOTES: (First Adventure)]

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>BLUE MACKEREL</i> (English mackerel) <i>Scomber australasicus</i> ... The blue mackerel is more slender than most tunas and is one of the smallest members of this family .. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,290,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] mackerel, English 2073-84 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> COMMON MACKEREL [<i>Scomber australasicus</i>] ... The names southern mackerel, English mackerel, and sometimes frigate mackerel are in use. (The latter properly belongs to another species.) <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,48</i>)

>  Luke was still out of work ... when the mackerel began to come in. He caught a few of the smaller type known locally as `English mackerel' ... One day, the other [fisherman] ... hauled in a fine `horse mackerel. <i>(1984,Some Other Country,150</i>) [NOTES: 1970 Middleton `The Loners' in 1984 Some Other Country 150]

>  <b>SORENSON</b> <i>MACKEREL</i> (two species) (a) Scientific name: (i) <i>Scomber australasicus </i>(ii) <i>Trachurus declivis</i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: (i) Blue Mackerel (ii) Jack mackerel. (c) Other common names: (i) Common Mackerel, Frigate mackerel, English mackerel (ii) Horse Mackerel. (d) Principal localities where taken: (both) Gisborne, Nelson, Picton ... <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,34</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> MACKEREL ... The common Mackerel is also known as the English Mackerel, Frigate Mackerel or Southern Mackerel. The Horse Mackerel is also known as the Yellowtail. The Koheru is also sometimes known as Yellowtail and is often misnamed Horse Mackerel and Scad. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,162</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Blue mackerel Scomber autralasicus</i> An Indo-Pacific species ... Often simply called mackerel, from its close similarity to the common mackerel (<i>Scomber scomber</i>) of the Northern Hemisphere ... In New Zealand it has often been called English mackerel and Pacific mackerel, but it is becoming more widely and appropriately known now as blue mackerel. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,124</i>)

>  [species name] blue mackerel .. [scientific name] <i>Scomber australasicus</i> .. [other name(s)] English mackerel, common mackerel, frigate mackerel, pacific mackerel, southern mackerel <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)



## English rat
 
 <i>n.</i> the introduced European rat

>  <b>HAAST</b> It has become a common saying among the Maories, that as the English rat (kiore Pakeha) has destroyed the native rat, so in time will the Maori vanish before the white man. These English rats are to be met with everywhere. <i>(1861,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,135</i>)

## enrichment
 
 <i>n.</i> the process of adding sugar to grapes during fermentation to improve the quality of wine

>  ...adding sugar is by no means off limits at the front end of the winemaking process... Here in New Zealand it goes by the wider term enrichment, which can include the addition not just of sugar, but also extra grape juice or concentrated grape juice. <i>(May 15, 2002,Capital Times,21</i>)

 <i>n.</i> an area from which schools may take pupils

>  This means that once [a school] establishes an enrolment zone, pupils from outside that zone will have to make a special application to be able to attend, and a ballot may be necessary to decide on some of these applications. <i>(Jun 11, 2001,Northern Courier,1</i>)

## ensilage
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The best ensilage I have ever seen came from a rapidly-filled silo. <i>(Jan 1, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,29</i>)

>  <b>BROWN</b> Ensilage! Years ago in Queensland I first heard the word. <i> Ibid. 63</i> Into Bulls, cow town, and I could still smell cow dung or ensilage or both.<i>Ibid.</i> 64 Still can I recall the aromatic tobacco-like fragrance of the brown heaps of ensilage, when the thrice-blessed silos were broached.. <i>(1939,Farmer's Wife, The,63</i>)

>  The ensilage was not in the pit merely as part of the usual quota deemed sufficient by most farmers for winter feeding out. <i>(Feb 7, 1946,NZ Farmers' Weekly,10</i>)

>  Into Bulls, cow town, and I could still smell cow dung or ensilage or both. <i>(Sep 1966,Landfall,20(3), No. 79/236</i>)

>  To make ensilage properly, the crop should not be allowed to get over-ripe. <i>(1894,Ann. Rep. Dept of Agriculture,II/178</i>)

>  An exchange asks "does ensilage produce good milk?" We have never tasted ensilage milk, our cows having produced all we require. <i>(Apr 25, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  <b>HAWKE & SCOTT</b> The 'New Zealand Farmer' in 1889 claimed to be the first journal in New Zealand to draw attention to the making of ensilage by simply stacking it in an open paddock. <i>(1985,Early Farm Machinery in NZ,101</i>)

>  What crops into ensilage? Practically anything that is green and succulent. <i>(Dec 18, 1926,NZ Dairy Produce Exporter,26</i>)

>  <b>CONNELL & HADFIELD</b> [Caption] The modern Wheeled-Sweep, eminently suitable for ensilage, for which it is here being used. <i>Ibid.</i> 379 Ensilage is superior to haymaking under many circumstances because it allows of surplus feed to be dealt with when it should be dealt with. <i>(1940,Agriculture,377,5th ed.</i>)

>  To the farmers of New Zealand there is no subject more deserving of consideration than the conservation of fodder by means of ensilage. <i>(Jan 1912,NZ Journal of Agriculture,7</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The 'Taranaki method' of ensilage stacking - stacking grass, green oats on anything handy, and weighing it by earth excavated from the side of the stack, the earth being afterwards replaced when the silage was being used. <i>(Oct 17, 1903,Farmers' Advocate,5</i>)

>  During last month the ensilage instructor... conducted demonstrations on several Otago farms. <i>(Feb 1912,NZ Journal of Agriculture,105</i>)

## Entebbe Operation
 
 <i>phr.</i> siting a team at a foreign sports venue only long enough to complete (successfully) their events.

>  [R]owing manager and fund-raiser Dudley Storey is planning another `Entebbe (hit and run) operation' and expects his charges to live outside of the village at next August's Los Angeles Olympics. `We'll continue with the Entebbe style of operation of flying in, doing the job and flying out again,' said Aucklander Storey ... `It suits us. We don't have the money to do it any other way, and we've proved this year and last that we can come in just before and still be successful. <i>(Sep 22, 1983,Dominion,20/5</i>)

## entire-leaved beech
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Nothofagus solandri</i> [ENTIRE-LEAVED BEECH (BIRCH)] (See [beech](../B#beech))

>  <b>KIRK</b> With the view of obviating the confusion caused by the misuse of the common names hitherto applied to the New Zealand beeches, I would suggest the adoption of new names based on the obvious characteristics of their foliage:- For <i>Fagus fusca</i>, tooth-leaved beech; for <i>Fagus solandri</i>, entire-leaved beech; and for <i>Fagus menziesii</i>, round-leaved beech. <i>(1875,Durability NZ Timbers,17</i>)

>  The utmost confusion prevails among the common names of the birches. There are scarcely two districts, a few miles apart, in which the same name is applied to the same tree, and a similar result may be obtained by consulting two bushmen in the same bush. With a view to obviating this difficulty Mr Kirk suggests `the adoption of new names based of the obvious characteristics of foliage [e.g.] for Fagus fusca `tooth-leaved beech'; for Fagus solandri `entire-leaved beech' etc. [This is unsuitable as foliage varies] I would suggest the retention of the common Otago names Fagus fusca - Red birch ; F. solandri - Black-heart birch ; F Menziesii - Silver birch. <i>(1876,TrNZI,6/4</i>) [NOTES: (Blair)]

>  <b>KIRK</b> <i>(1889,Forest Flora</i>) [NOTES: (And)]

>  Beech, entire leaved .. Fagus Solandri .. Heavy, tough, strong timber; used for sleepers, fencing, bridges, &c. <i>Ibid.</i> 16 35 Hand specimen, entire leaved beech (black heart birch). <i>(1890,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,9</i>)

>  The area of heavy forest on the eastern side of the South Island is small; the inland Oxford and Alford Forests consisting chiefly of entire-leaved beech. <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,119</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Beech</i> ... In New Zealand, there are six species of true beeches, which according to Kirk are as follows - ... ENTIRE-LEAVED B. <i>F. Solandri</i>, Hook. f. ... All these, however, are commonly called <i>Birches. </i> <i>(1898,Austral English,24</i>)

>  <i>(1908,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: (And) Cockayne Botanical Survey Tongariro National Park]

>  <i>(1910,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: (And)Cockayne NZ Plants and Their Story]

>  <i> [Notho]fagus solandri</i>, Hook. f. ... <i>Myrtilloides cinerascens,</i> Banks & Sol., MS. <i>Cliffortiordes oblongata,</i> Dryander. Entire-leaved Beech. Black Birch of Wellington, Canterbury, and in part of Otago and Southland. White Birch of Nelson, and in part Otago. Black-heart Birch of Otago. White, Black, Red, and Brown Birch of Oxford and Alford Forests. A fine tree, sometimes attaining the height of 100 feet, but usually 60-80 feet, with a trunk from 2 to 4 feet or more in diameter.] <i>(1884,TrNZI,17/298</i>) [NOTES: (Kirk, NZ Beeches)]

## entree
 
 <i>n.</i> in New Zealand, a dish served before the main course.

>  <b>GREIG & HADDAD</b> entree | FRENCH | In New Zealand an entree is usually a dish served before the main course. In a full French menu it is served between the fish and the roast. In America the entree is usually the main course of a meal. <i>(2000,NZ Cook's Dictionary, The,147</i>)

>  The dishes are fresh, interesting and very good value with entrees from $8 to $16 and the main courses mainly priced under $20. [108] His menu is short - just six entrees, four mains and four puddings... <i>(Jul 1995,Cuisine,No. 51/104</i>)

>  From a brief range of entrees, I choose duck liver pate; he gets portobello mushroom ($12 each). <i>(Sep 21, 2003,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),17</i>)

## Enz, the
 
 <i>n.</i> short for Split Enz, a New Zealand pop group [also Enz attrib.]
