>  They were pretty romantic times, the early Enz. <i>(Feb 4, 1991,Listener (New Zealand Listener),74</i>)

>  Never the last to leave the party, Split Enz have begun to dabble in styles new to them. <i>Ibid.</i> 9 The other distinctive feature of Split Enz has always been their very visual stage act, which in England has extended to some of the audience, known as Frenz of the Enz, dressing in like fashion. <i>(Sep 1977,Rip It Up (Auckland),7</i>)

>  By then Tim had left Sacred Heart College and was flatting in Auckland with like-minded friends who were shortly to form a "folksy combo" which they first called Split Ends and later patriotised to Split Enz... The Split Enz song "Six Months In A Leaky Boat" was making it onto British radio in 1982 until the Falklands War broke out, when the song was removed from the airwaves. <i>(Oct 1997,North & South,133</i>)

>  When Split Enz split up in December 1984, some obeservers predicted it wouldn't be too long before the members of the band began playing together again... With this month's national tour by Enz offshoots Crowded House and Schnell Fenster, the 16-year saga of this country's most successful band comes full circle. <i>(Jan 21, 1989,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32</i>)

>  Finn and Judd became the creative impetus behind the first incarnation of Split Enz, perhaps New Zealand's most recognisable rock group... Instead of using the band's 1985 recording with the New Zealand Symphony Orchestra, known as ENZSO, as a starting point, Balwin opted for a much more raw approach to convey in dance the Enz times... Baldwin likens the Enz approach to that taken by Igor Stravinsky - a composer he says had the reputation of being a classical music magpie. <i>(Jun 7, 2001,Dominion,19</i>)

>  "I'm here to see Tim Finn from that great Aussie band Split Enz," she says, smirking. <i>(Mar 2, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),52</i>)

>  He can enjoy Enz reunions... <i>(Apr 27, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),50</i>)

 <i>n.</i> New Zealand (esp. in WW1 and WW2, and like Anzac, Kiwi prob. first applied as a distinguishing epithet by non-New Zealanders).

>  Pronunciation of the abbreviation as two letters rather than two words appears to have developed in the early 20th century, giving rise to a new written form <i>Enzed</i> (also <i>En-Zed, En Zed</i>), which was first recorded (along with <i>Enzedder</i>) in 1917. <i>(Aug 2000,NZWords,4 : 1</i>)

## equalisator
 
 <i>n.</i> a system of betting on races in which numbers are not allocated to horses until after betting on a race has closed, thereby ensuring that everyone has an equal chance of winning. [Jim Cameron says: Not in DNZE or any other dictionary I have seen.]

>  Wagering at Bulls was formerly a matter of "sweeps" and "side-wagers" until Messrs Hill and Poole installed their "Equalisator" in the 80's. <i>(1957,Racing in Rangitikei,24</i>)

>  <b>MORTIMER</b> [At Rawene in the 1950s, the racetrack did not have a totalisator, but it did have an equalisator. He explains that] "If you picked the winner, you doubled your money, but there were no returns for picking horses which came second or third." <i>(2000,From a Distance: The Waima River Years,82</i>)

## ergot
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a small horny growth on the fetlock of horses.

>  The animal exhibited large horny adventitious appendages of the skin projecting laterally from the posterior portion of the fetlocks; the small horny growths popularly known as "the ergots", and usually found in the coarse hair at the back of the fetlock, being absent in this case. ..apart from the unusual character of the growths seen in this particular case, it may perhaps be of interest to recall that "ergots" represent the functionless verstige of the once large callous pad to be seen in this situation in the tapir and rhinoceros ... <i>(Feb 15, 1912,NZ Journal of Agriculture,94</i>)

>  .. the large callous pad being represented by the small "ergots." ['the ergots'] <i>(Feb 15, 1912,NZ Journal of Agriculture,95</i>)



## ergotised
 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of grass] affected by black ergot.

>  Cattle should be removed from ergotized paddocks and be given a complete change of feed. <i>(Apr 20, 1922,NZ Journal of Agriculture,253</i>)

>  Hay made from ergotised grass will cause lameness and sometimes even loss of feet in cattle. [ergotized] <i>(Jun 1956,NZ Journal of Agriculture,15</i>)



## ergoty
 
 <i>adj.</i> Of sheep, and fig., affected by or as by ergot; subject to violent fits. (Occas. applied to grasses (e.g.fescue, or cocksfoot) showing signs of ergot.)

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> What yer goin' on like an ergitty sheep for. <i>(1905,Sons o' Men,129</i>)

## eschcholtzia
 
 <i>n.</i> [<i>E. californica</i> as a profuse and beautiful introduced weed of southern NZ riverbeds, remarkable for the difficulty of its spelling.] =Californian poppy,.

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> CALIFORNIAN POPPY (<i>Eschsholzia [Eschscholtzia] californica</i>), often called eschscholtzia, and sometimes Johnny-go-to-bed, on account perhaps of its nightcap-like calyces, is found in profusion on the shingly river-beds of the South Island. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,86,1967 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> People had lived here [at Kawarau, Central Otago] once, a [53] sizeable community, and their traces showed in the wraiths of runaway gardens, white flags, marigolds, and escholtzias [sic] growing without rhyme or reason. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,52</i>)

>  <b>RHODES</b> Beneath him as he drove slowly across the musical planking of a wooden bridge a brilliant mass of golden eschscholtzias springing from the shingle laid a carpet of pure colour to contain the bright brown waters of the Selwyn River. <i>(1952,Fly Away Peter,99</i>)

>  <b>SLAQTTER</b> The Taylor river-fan glowing with eschscholtzia, the orange-yellow four-o'-clock, orbright with blue borage ... <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,171</i>)

>  eschscholtzia [s] Eschscholtzia californica Cham. <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,24</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> ESCHSCHOLZIA (Eschscholzia californica) known also as Californian poppy. ... Correctly spelled without t, although named after Eschscholtz. <i>(1981,Weeds of Roadsides,47</i>)

## esplanade reserve
 
 <i>n.</i> a strip of land along the foreshore, lake margins and river banks required to be set aside on any subdivision of land to protect conservation values and give public access for recreational use. [See Resource Management Act 1991 ss 229ff.] (See [Queen's Chain](../Q#queen's-chain))

>  Esplanade reserves work in the reversemanner - the setting aside of one chainalong mean high water mark, the marginsof lakes, rivers and streams over a widthof 10 ft. being mandatory in the case ofsubdivisions in a County. <i>(1969,Coastal Development,120</i>)

>  In counties, owners of coastal lakes andriverside land who subdivide it intosections smaller than 10 acres arenormally required to dedicate a strip ofland above the foredune for access anduse by the public. The minimum width ofthis strip of so-called "esplanadereserve" is generally 66 feet (one chain)measured from mean high water mark. <i>(1972,Coastal Development,49</i>)

>  "You could wander along the water's edge and move from a marginal strip (controlled by DOC) to a "paper road" or esplanade reserve controlled by the district council, then onto privately owned land and be unable to detect any differences. <i>(Mar 11, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),48</i>)

>  The other, crowning irony is that had the coast been subdivided, the council could realistically have requested esplanade reserves that guaranteed access. <i>(May 12, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),35</i>)

>  The Resource Management Act provision to set aside Esplanade Reserves is useless, as councils do not want the financial burden of paying survey purchase and maintenance costs of the land to be set aside, and most land fronting waterways and the coast will never be subdivided, so they would never be contiguous. <i>(Feb 23, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),8</i>)

>  When land beside the coast or a river is subdivided, councils can claim up to 20m from the high water mark as esplanade reserve - the so-called Queen's chain. <i>(Jul 20, 2002,Weekend Herald,B7</i>)

>  Among other things, he says Makara Ohariu residents are worried that the public could get access to esplanade strips on their land as of right. <i>(Oct 4, 2000,Norwester,1</i>)

>  [Caption] Overseer for the Riversdale esplanade reserve upgrade. <i>(Jun 13, 2000,Wairarapa Midweek,00</i>)



## esplanade strip
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Two additions are being presented as improvements, the first being esplanade strips. These may be created if a subdivision is made but, unlike the current esplanade reserves, they remain the property of the landowner (esplanade reserves are vested in the territorial authority). <i>(Apr 3, 1993,Listener (New Zealand Listener),12</i>)

## eucalyptus
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SHEPHERD</b> Some of the timber trees are of large growth, particularly three sorts; the first is of the pine tribe and is known by the name of Pimise (Pinus??) in three varieties on the north. The second is something like the Lucombe Oack, and the third is called the Red Gum or Eucalipticus I have seen it in England. ... The three varieties of pine are very tall and handsome, and one in appearance like the Lucombe oak grows tall but not so straight, the Eucalipticy grows to a great thickeness in the stem and frequently not less than fifteen feet in girt but not more than 15 or 20 feet in height before it spreads its large branches in every direction, ... <i>(1826,Journal</i>) [NOTES: 1826 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 356]



## euchre
 
 <i>v.</i> do (a person) out of (something); outdo, defeat.

>  <b>PYKE</b> See here, ... this is our claim, and I don't mean to be euchred out of it. <i>(1873,Wild Will Enderby,1/v/20,1889,1974 ed.</i>)

>  `Considering the time he has been at the game it is simply marvellous to see the way he euchres other riders. He seems to be able to instil confidence into uncertain horses, an d by thismeans to get them to go kindly for him.' <i>(May 14, 1910,NZ Truth,255/2/6</i>)

>  euchre v. defeat, foil, outwit. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),8</i>)

>  "You'd euchre it properly," warned Jumbo, "you don't know anything about watches." <i>(Jan 10, 1944,NZEF Times,III/133 : 10</i>)

>  They came to the conclusion that the sheep were lousy, and to "euchre" the Inspector.. it would be better to kill the whole flock. <i>(Oct 9, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)



## euchre party
 
 <i>n.</i> An organised series of euchre games to determine overall winners.

>  <b>WOOLLASTON</b> ... the table [in the Huinga public hall] they used as the King table at the Wednesday night euchre parties became an altar. <i>(1980,Sage Tea,41</i>) [NOTES: [c.WW1, Taranaki]]

>  The large dining room was fitted up with small tables and a progressive euchre party arranged for the older folk. <i>(Aug 6, 1919,Alexandra Herald,5</i>)



## euchred
 
 <i>adj.</i> Exhausted; done in; ruined.

>  <b>MARSHALL NALDER</b> They were euchred by the bolting of the mules. <i>(1900,Battle-smoke Ballads,14</i>)

>  <b>SMYTH</b> Damn good thing he's euchred. <i>(1928,Jean of the Tussock Country,200</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> I bet the old girl was a bit euchred by the time you got her home again, eh? <i>(1965,Open Country,91</i>)

>  Humphries is chuckling over the clever way in which he has euchred the Law Society. <i>(Aug 30, 1890,NZ Observer,6</i>)

>  Eddie euchred a turntable's magnetic cartridge when he started last year and has only recently been allowed to play records again. <i>(Apr 21, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),65</i>)

>  There is a strong probability that theman who has accustomed himself to takingfrom the whisky decanter a shilling'sworth for sixpence will find himself"euchred" in the Dunedin bars. <i>(Dec 4, 1916,Stratford Evening Post,4</i>)

## European
 
 <i>n.</i> a non-Maori (orig. immigrant) white resident of New Zealand; a Pakeha

>  <b>BIDWILL & WOODHOUSE</b> One of the newest features of the valley is the appearance of cottages belonging to the wives and children of European settlers. <i>(1927,Bidwill of Pihautea,9</i>) [NOTES: Quotation from Weld (1849)]

>  Of the 16, five were Polynesian, four Maori and seven European. "That's a remarkably high figure for Europeans because it's [sc. sharing accomodation] not an automatic cultural option forthem." <i>(May 8, 1992,Dominion,9</i>)

>  <b>BOWMAN</b> As I had no further business in that part of the country, and knew from charts, that New Zealand (as it is called by the Europeans) is a great extent of country. <i>(1778,Travels of Hildebrand Bowman,29</i>)

>  <b>NICHOLAS</b> .. that species of loathsome vermin to which uncleanly Europeans have long been familiarized, were crawling over them [sc. New Zealanders] in myriads. These vermin, which are too well known among us to require their name being repeated, are in their language called cootoos, and the inhabitants in general are equally full of them.] <i>Ibid.</i> 2/338 A white man, (the flea is also called by this name, as they assert it to have been introduced intotheir country by Europeans - the turnip is likewise called packaha[^] from its whiteness) Packaha[^] <i>(1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/87</i>)

>  <b>MARSDEN</b> When our arrival was known we were soon surrounded by numbers of the natives, who vied withone another in their attentions. None of us were under the smallest apprehensions of danger, any more than if amongst Europeans. <i>(1815,Letters & Journals of Samuel Marsden,96,1932 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ELDER</b> The natives approve of Europeans settling amongst them, through motives of self-interest. <i>(1934,Marsden's Lieutenants,141</i>) [NOTES: 1817 Jul 25 Kendall in Elder Marsden's Lieutenants]

>  <b>CRUISE</b> [cp native] During the week the Europeans employed in the woods received little or no assistance from the natives, who in general were planting their potatoes; ... <i>(1823,Journal,163,1957 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1820 Sep 24 in 1823 Cruise Journal (1957)]

>  <b>HAY</b> The flax settlements will rapidly assume a more permanent form, as the present desultory and speculative system progressively fails ... [10] F.Mascetu - a flax settlement; four Europeans here; likely to be permanent ... <i>(1832,Notices of NZ,7</i>)

## European flax
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Linum</i>....... linen flax

>  <b>ELDER</b> [?English linen flax] I had also sent over a little English flax seed. This also had been sown and come to great perfection. <i>(1814,Marsden's Lieutenants,100</i>) [NOTES: (Griffiths Collection)]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> .. an experiment has been tried [at New Plymouth] ... of growing Linum, or European flax. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,281</i>)

>  ... separated milk may be restored to its original value for feeding purposes ... by the addition of linseed mucilage, and therefore an acre or so of European flax should be grown on every farm where stock-rearing is carried on. <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,127</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Flax, Native, n</i>. The European flax is <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>, N.O. <i>Linea</i>. There is a species in Australia, <i>Linum marginale</i>, Cunn., N.O. <i>Linacea</i>, called Native Flax, In New Zealand, the <i>Phormium</i> is called <i>Native Flax</i>. See next word [<i>Flax, New Zealand</i>]. <i>(1898,Austral English,147</i>)

## ewe block
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  As every ewe block had a set of yards and a mothering paddock, ewes and lambs were mustered in sections, with very early starts to avoid the summer heat. <i>(1952,My Yesteryears,21</i>) [NOTES: [f. c1869]]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a large land area where breeding ewes are grazed.

>  <b>BLAKISTON</b> As every ewe block had a set of yards and a mothering paddock, ewes and lambs were mustered in sections ... <i>(1952,My Yesteryears,21</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> By following a policy of shifting the ewes on to alternate blocks every so many years, the country is not subject to the almost continual grazing which is usually the case with ewe blocks. <i>(1952,High Country Journey,160</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Immediately after that, we straggled the ewe blocks ... <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,32</i>)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> The ewe blocks on Mount Algidus are not paddocks. <i>(1966,Over the River,84</i>)

>  <b>WHEELER</b> Country facing the lake, the winter ewe block. <i>(1971,Historic Sheep Stations SI II,35</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> Our job was to ride the ewe blocks or paddocks checking to see if any ewes were cast. <i>(1996,I Was So Lucky,4</i>)



## ewe country
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>STRONACH</b> The next job was mustering the ewe country, and we began to prepare. --> ibid. 25 The Molesworth ewe country has a reputation. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,25</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the better pastoral land on a station property [ being safe to graze in all seasons and keeping ewes in sound breeding condition.]

>  The figures given serve to emphasize the point that the ewe country is small in area. <i>(Feb 20, 1937,NZ Journal of Agriculture,99</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> And the Ewe Country seemed not so bad to me as we ran out our mob of 6,00 ewes into a paddock near the station. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,29</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> .. for shorn sheep to scatter over the ewe country at Molesworth would have taken weeks. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,31</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> This is the station's ewe country, Ngamatea itself being used mainly for the carrying of dry stock, and of course as summer country. <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,151</i>)

>  <b>BECKETT The Mountains of Erewhon</b> 135 It was not considered suitable ewe country ... <i>(1978,Mountains of East Asia and the Pacific</i>)



## ewe equivalent
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a feed potential or requirement measure [also known as stock unit.]

>  We hear and read a lot today about an expression 'ewe equivalents.' <i>(Dec 24, 1964,NZ Farmer,28</i>)

>  This gives a total stocking rate of 7.6 ewe equivalents. <i>(May 8, 1969,NZ Farmer,30</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The fully-developed areas are estimated to have a capacity of over five ewe equivalents ... <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,226</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> ewe equivalent: a very modern expression used to denote the capacity per acre or hectare of country or pasture to carry stock. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,29</i>)



## ewe paddock
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a paddock with quality feed, kept particularly for grazing ewes prior to and during lambing. (See [lambing paddock](../L#lambing-paddock))

## Executive Council
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> Under the Amended Licensing Ordinance Session III., No. 21, 1844, `Bush Licences' aregranted for the convenience of Travellers, at rates fixed by the Governor and Executive Council,according to the traffic of the place. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,195</i>)

>  Executive Council Governor-General and Ministers that give formal approval to enactments <i>(1985,Collins Compact Dictionary,170</i>) [NOTES: Collins Concise Eng.Dict. 387; nf. 1986 NZPOD]

>  <i>(1893,AJHR,A-7A/38</i>)

>  The Executive Council are the men who have for years headed the popular party unanimous in its demands for the Land Bills. <i>(1893,AJHR,A-7A/38</i>)

>  <b>WEBB</b> New Zealand received a governor of its own [sc. in 1840], an executive council, and a legislative council. <i>(1940,Government in NZ,4</i>)

>  Pursuant to the Public Safety Conservation Act, 1932, there being a Proclamation of Emergency in Force.. His Excellency the Governor General, acting by and with the advice and consent of the Executive Council, doth hereby make the following regulations. <i>(1951,Statutory Regulations,24/65</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> The Executive Council is comprised of the Governor-General, who presides, and all the members of Parliament whom he has appointed as Ministers. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,I/849</i>)

>  <b>SCOTT</b> The function of Cabinet is to decide policy, the function of the Executive Council to tender advice to the Governor- General when the law requires advice to be tendered in this way, for instancewhen advice is tendered that an Order-in-Council be issued... Cabinet and the Executive Council are two bodies with the same membership and different functions. <i>(1962,NZ Constitution,78</i>)

>  <b>BATEMAN</b> Executive Council is the legal entity which formally transforms decisions of Cabinet into law. <i>(1989,Illustrated Encycl. of NZ,379</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> On 12 February 1858, Stafford laid before the Executive Council a memorandum by the Ministers on the state of affairs in Taranaki. <i>Ibid.</i> 132 It was noticeable that, whereas the [Taranaki] Provincial Council wanted Maori owners to be enabled to sell.. the Government implied that a majority wanted to sell. Ibid. 139 The Governor hitherto had seemed to understand that Maori land tenure was communal, as he had emphasized in June 1858 when refusing the Taranaki Provincial Council's petition that he compel a division of the common land. <i>(1957,Origins of the Maori Wars,129</i>)

## Exodus
 
 <i>n.</i> [hist. usu. as "the Exodus."]

>  <b>SOLJAK</b> The seventeen years' depression between 1876 and 1893 was marked by a movement known to New Zealanders as `the exodus,' when over 150,000 people, or approximately 25 per cent of the total population, migrated from New Zealand to Australia. <i>(1946,New Zealand (various authors/titles),52</i>)

>  Unemployed workers unsuccessfully petitioned the President of the United States in 1880, and the House of Representatives in Victoria in 1885, for assistance to migrate. During the late eighties the departures exceeded the arrivals by many thousands. During `the Exodus', as it was called, most of the emigrants went to Victoria, ... <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,159</i>)



## exotic
 
 <i>n.</i> an introduced plant or animal

>  If stock is excluded, there's a risk the native flora will be taken over by naturalised exotics. <i>(Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/90</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> In NZ, now usu. with reference to an introduced as distinct from a native tree (or forest), esp. Pinus radiata. cf.

>  ... every description of fern, whether native or exotic, ... <i>(1894,TrNZI,26/622</i>)

>  <b>LEE</b> Across the plains ran belts of exotics. <i>(1936,Hunted, The,50</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> In the country back towards Auckland and civilization, north of Arapuni, there had been a conquest by exotics; close-growing old puriri trees disputed the soil with little plantations of pine, stream willows were russet in autumn, and fawn-coloured poplars cleft straight upwards. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,191</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> The region is noted for its indigenous trees and also for exotics planted for shelter or for farm firewood and timber. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,16,1970 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ARMFELT</b> `But here on the Flat almost all the trees were exotic: the windbelts of radiata pine or macrocarpa ... (it sounded peculiar to hear them called `exotic'): the oaks and the ash-trees <i>(1971,Catching Up,20</i>)

>  <b>LOCKLEY</b> ... a steep bluff clothed in a mixture of native and exotic bush, ... <i>(1980,House Above the Sea,3</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>adj.</i> In collocations. exotic forest, exotic pine.

>  <b>SOLJAK</b> These exotic forest resources [insignis pine, Douglas fir, etc.] are being expanded to ... facilitate preservation of the native forests, which the early settlers wastefully reduced ... <i>(1946,New Zealand (various authors/titles),11</i>)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> Anyhow, what eventually happened to that great wild stallion, will probably always remain just another of the secrets of the huge exotic forests of the wild and lonely Rangitaiki Plains. <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,193</i>)

>  <b>CHRISTIE</b> It [Tarawera Hotel] nestled on a flat piece of ground between hills devoid of any growth except for a thin line of exotic pines between the hotel and the horse paddock. <i>(1987,Candles and Canvas,79</i>)

## expert
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a person in a shearing gang who maintains machinery and equipment in a shed [and who sharpens gang members' combs and cutters].

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> When the main shearing was in progress, one man was employed as the expert. His job was to grind all the combs and cutters for the ten shearers, grease and oil all the overheads and run the engine. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,17</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. only the woolclasser and the expert - the man who maintained the machines - were given hacks to ride. <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,26</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> No shearer was allowed into the engine and grinding room as this work was all done by an expert. <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,43</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> Shaddow Waru, the expert, was busy in the engine room getting the Lister running smoothly. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,25</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to maintain shearing equipment and machinery in a woolshed.

>  <b>BOWEN</b> The next phase of experting a new comb is to scrape or rub the tips on wood. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,74</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> A term for the person who grinds the shearing gear, and keeps the handpieces and plant in order [(shearers')]

>  <b>CARR</b> The boss Maori undertakes to supply all the rouseabouts (as shed hands are termed) i.e., fleece pickers, wool rollers and `Sheep oh!' the [sic] owner or manager generally gets his own pressers and expert (where machines are run). <i>(1913,Country Work,14</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> EXPERT. The man who grinds the gear, and keeps the handpieces and plant in order. This manis often a retired shearer. <i>(1956,Wool Away!,156</i>) [NOTES: glossary: Shearing Terms]

>  Expert: Australian term for the person who grinds the shearing gear, and keeps the handpieces and plant in order. Often a retired shearer. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,55</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i>

>  To Shear and Expert `That Remit No. 7 be struck out, i.e. - ... `That no shearer be allowed to shear and expert on the same board.' ... `That no shearer expert and shear on a board of more than five shearers.' <i>(1912,NZ Shearers (Report Third Annual Conference),11</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> The next phase of experting a new comb is to scrape or rub the tips on wood. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,74</i>)



## experting
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>BOWEN</b> Unfortunately, one sees far too many of these sharp-pointed butchering combs. I trust that the experting as set out in this text will be a practical guide and help to all, as good gear is the foundation of all good shearing. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,75</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> ... he was always `ringer' of the gang besides doing the experting. <i>(1986,Off the Sheep's Back,121</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the maintaining of shearing equipment and machinery in a woolshed.

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> He was always 'ringer' of the gang besides doing the experting. <i>(1986,Off the Sheep's Back,121</i>)

>  <b>SALISBURY</b> The chap who did the experting when I was there took his responsibilities very seriously. No one was allowed in the engine room and hardly allowed to look at the Lister, which of course at any other time was very much part of the station. <i>(1994,I Was So Lucky,17</i>)



## export mutton
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) sheep-meat that is considered to be of a high grade.

>  <b>KERR</b> A Romney ewe is a most important unit on most farms. Crossed with a fat-lamb sire, her prime lambs form eighty per cent of the export market and as an aged girl she becomes part of that, at times, high-priced 'export mutton.' <i>(1976,From the Beginning: chronicles of a county,136</i>)



## export of brains
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>LIPSON</b> Yet persistently New Zealand refuses opportunities to its most talented sons and daughters, denied them the chance of creative expression, and often drives them from its own shores in the annual `export of brains' which is its greatest tragedy. <i>(1948,Politics of Equality, The,493</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> It may seem that science is something that very intelligent people go abroad to pursue, a process which in England has recently been called the `brain drain' and has in New Zealand ... been called the `export of brains' for at least twenty years. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,85</i>)



## express
 
 <i>n.</i> Ellipt. for express wagon. [Obs.]

>  <b>DAVIDSON</b> The station [sc. sheep station] express was got out ... and away drove Duncan ... <i>(1889,Stories of NZ Life,5</i>)

>  <b>GRACE</b> The man ... ran round to the back of his `express' - a vehicle not unlike a square tray on four wheels. <i>(1914,Tale of a Timber Town,69</i>)

>  <b>35</b> He had hitherto not heard the term `express' applied to these tempestuous vehicles. <i>(Feb 10, 1921,Quick March</i>)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> My father was able to get Harry Stone, a very good chap, to do the driving of the express, the farmwork and the blacksmithing. <i>(1968,Remembered Trails,29</i>)

>  <b>PRESTON</b> Mother and father, we three children ... travelled in convoy in two buggies, a light one with small, fast horses and a large `express' pulled by half-draughts. <i>(1978,Woolgatherers,104</i>) [NOTES: in 1901]

>  <b>NEAVE</b> [Caption to picture showing an `express' - a 4-wheeled open spring wagon with raised sides, and moveable front wheels : seating can be put in.] T.A.Munro's Stables ... The dark horse in the express is a young horse being broken in. ... <i>Ibid..</i> 46 The present day holidaying by car and caravan was anticipated by my parents [ca 1900], who used a covered express and five horses to travel to Central Otago, Lakes Wanaka and Wakatipu, and Queenstown. <i>(1980,Land of Munros, The,42</i>)



## express wagon
 
 <i>n.</i> (obsolete) Orig. (see OED2 express a. etc C. 3 cit.1860) a wagon in which packages etc. were taken to and from an express office; in NZ commonly a light four-wheeled horse-drawn sprung vehicle usu., but not exclusively, for goods delivery. cf Yankee wagon.

>  <b>THATCHER</b> There was a little express wagon waiting to convey these Melbourne notables to Dunedin. <i>(1862,Dunedin Songster,1/8</i>)

>  <b>BRADSHAW</b> Our `buggies', a generic name in the colony for every description of vehicle on springs short of the `express' waggon, were carefully overhauled. <i>(1883,New Zealand As It Is,81</i>)



## expressman
 
 <i>n.</i> The driver of an express wagon.

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> One of my brothers was an expressman in early Dunedin ... my brother was engaged to take the luggage from the house to the jetty. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,180</i>)

## extra
 
 <i>adv.</i> [Prob. a shortening of extraordinar(il)y.] Frequent in speech intensifying a following aj with a sense `excellently', or in collocations extra early (obs.), extra curly (see curly), extra good [1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) extra nice]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> extra early. First rate; very good, excellent: New Zealand: since ca. 1945. <i>(1961,Dictionary of Slang,1081</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> We do not know whether an affable New Zealander about to agree to something is going to say extra good or good as gold or simply all right or even O.K. [177] Recent popular expressions are good as gold or extra good, both meaning simply `good'. Good as gold is an affable way of saying `Right you are'. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,30, 177</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> extra enhancing intensive which can reverse the meaning of the parent word, as in the first of the two common uses of it following: - extra curly first rate; eg `That last four Martin Crowe belted was extra curly'; c.1935. [P8: nf] - extra grouse exceptionally attractive; eg `The spread put on by Ken's Mum after the game was extra grouse.' Often applied to a desired woman; possibly derived from the gamebird `grouse'. [P8: Aus] <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,43</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  In case you were wondering what your adolescent daughter was really trying to tell you: Gs means girlfriends, budget means of less than acceptable quality and extras refers to people who butt in on conversations uninvited. <i>(Dec 5, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15</i>)



## extra curly
 
 <i>adj.</i> particularly good [cf curly]

>  <b>Partridge</b> curly ... Difficult, as in `That's a curly one' ...(?mostly) Aus.: since ca. 11950. ...2. But in NZ as , e.g., `That was extra curly' (Slatter), it seems to mean `excellent' or `attractive' and to date since ca. 1935. <i>(1984,Dictionary of Slang,280</i>)

>  Winning the reference and anthology section of the New Zealand Book Awards on Monday night was sweet as, corker, choice, beaut and extra curly. <i>(Jul 27, 2005,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.wanganuichronicle.co.nz/news/book-award-win-choice-says-dictionary-man/948487/]

>  I've won tickets to a couple of them so that's been extra curly. <i>(May 18, 2010,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://www.realwomen.co.nz/component/option,com_fireboard/Itemid,60/func,view/id,41448/view,threaded/catid,2/]

## extravaganza
 
 <i>n.</i> [Spec. and joc. use of extravaganza--> a composition, literary, musical, of an extravagant or fantastic character.] The traditional name of the Victoria University (College) student revue, beginning c1907 with `The Golden Calf'.

>  <b>FAIRBURN</b> Cheeking the examiner in an Economics Paper ... throwing a roll of toilet paper ... on to the stage from the Gods on the last night of the extravaganza. <i>(1926,Letters,5,1981 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BEAGLEHOLE</b> More uniformly satisfactory [than capping ceremonies] was what came to be called the `Carnival'. ... [128] ... Starting, therefore, with concerts of miscellaneous songs, hakas [etc] ... , the revellers went on to develop the `extravaganza' - or rather a number of different sorts of extravaganza; for no attempt to stabilize permanently the pattern of that combination of buffoonery and satire has ever been successful. <i>(1949,Victoria University College:,127</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> She had performed with distinction in the dramatic club and in the annual [Victoria University College] extravaganza. <i>(1956,Sweet White Wine,72</i>)

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> The barman slapped a fellow on the shoulder. `When are you going to give that suit back to the Varsity Extravaganza? ...' <i>(1965,Power of Joy,295</i>)

>  Thirty years ago I auditioned for my first university capping revue, the '69 Extravaganza... Vic's revues grew from a long tradition of Extravs filled with parody and bawdy humour. <i>(Dec 11, 1999,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

>  Agitators generally provoke demonstrations under the guise of 'Proceshes' and 'Extravs.' <i>(1966,Cappicade,9</i>)

>  <b>HARCOURT</b> The typical university extravaganza is a show apart, conforming to no finely drawn lines of difference, but usually savours more or musical comedy than anything else. <i>(1978,Dramatic Appearance, A,41</i>) [NOTES: Quoted from 1926 programme note]

>  The students of Victoria College put on a successful extravaganza in the Town Hall. <i>(Jun 5, 1935,NZ Free Lance,5</i>)

>  The Students' Association went further than a mere revival of the Extravaganza - they restored to it its traditional character, which is burlesque. <i>(1929,Spike,29(55) : 35</i>)

>  The Opera House has been the traditional home of extravaganza for years. <i>(Apr 16, 1952,Evening Post,11</i>)

>  In 1911 another change has taken place. The Extravanganza (yes, it really was called an Extrav. that year) has moved to the Concert Chamber. <i>(1949,Spike,81</i>)

>  Victoria College is now recovering from its extravaganza which was quite the best thing ever produced by the students. <i>(Jun 24, 1924,Otago Witness,30</i>)

>  That change meant students would never again be able to take time out for activities such as Victoria's legendary "extravaganzas", the traditional highlight of the May graduation festivities, which have since died. <i>(Oct 6, 1992,NewsVUW (Wellington),10</i>)

## eye
 
 <i>n.</i> The quality of being able to command or work sheep by gaze; the trait of a sheep dog to stare at a sheep; deep concentration on the movements of the sheep; esp. in the collocation show eye; cf eye dog, strong eye, plain eye. a. ????? [(farming) 1933 Acland]

>  <b>LILICO</b> These dogs had any amount of eye and style and yet they were powerful forcers with plenty of noise ... <i>(1934,Sheep Dog Memoirs,1</i>)

>  The dog shown on page 13 is a huntaway ... that showed a considerable amount of eye. ... ahuntaway ... that showed a considerable amount of eye. <i>Ibid..</i> 9 Should the pup fail to show `eye' after developing a keen liking for working the rubber ... [Check "a pup" or "the pup"] <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,2</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> (Glossary) Eye: Common to our heading dogs. This is the action of `setting' sheep - as some of our sporting dogs set game - and exercising extreme concentration on the sheep they happen to be working. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,168</i>)

>  Eye: Trait of a dog to stare at a sheep; deep concentration on the movements of the sheep. `Strong-eye' or `plain-eye' dogs. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,24</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> `He'd be better of [sic] a bit more `noise' if he was to do much work among the logs, but forstraight-out `eye' work he's a pretty nice dog. <i>(1956,Green Kiwi,115,1960 ed.</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> plain eye: describing a dog which does not show eye. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,62</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> stinking with eye Of a sheep dog, very strong-eyed.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> A very strong-eyed dog (`stinking' with eye) works sheep in a manner similar to a cat stalking a bird. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,168</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (of a sheepdog) fix or work sheep with the eye.

>  <b>STRONACH</b> At Molesworth in those days most of our heading dogs were the `plain-working' kind. They did not `eye' the sheep or exercise any mesmerism, as is done by the present-day trial dog. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,43</i>)

>  <b>RENNIE</b> When a heading dog first begins to eye sheep it is important to break the eye by calling it so that it looks at you. <i>(1985,Working Dogs,37</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a hypnotic fix of a sheepdog to control sheep or the quality in a dog to so control sheep.

>  Force..is different from eye, the dog's control of sheep by staring them in the face. <i>(Oct 21, 1933,Press, The  (Acland Glossary),15</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> Should the pup fail to show 'eye' after developing a keen liking for working the rubber, it is advisable to begin to command him to 'Stand' .. <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,9</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Stinking with eye, Rock 'set' them about six feet from the mouth of the pen. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,83</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> In breeding, if she went to a strong-eyed dog she threw pups with some eye, but with noise on a mob. <i>(1973,Boot in the Stirrup,9</i>)

>  Eye - the optical fix or hypnotic stare a heading dog has to get a 'grip' on the sheep. <i>(Sep 15, 1983,NZ Journal of Agriculture,11</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [Transf. use of Cornish dial. eye a mass of ore left in a mine to be worked when others are scarce; hence, a `plum', a titbit left to last. cf. OED2 n1 16 b.: AND 1865.] (take) pick the eyes out (of land) to buy or alienate the choicest pieces in a block, to spot. Contrast gridiron. [(obs. or hist.)]

>  <b>JOLLIE</b> [To Jollie's account [c1856] of buying the best parts out of a rented or leased run to preventsomeone else stepping in and buying the run near hanmer, A.J. Allom notes: This was probably about the beginning of the grid-iron process of picking the eyes out of country, so much practised afterwards' <i>(1872,Reminiscences,39</i>) [NOTES: c1872 A.J. Allom's note to Jollie Reminiscences 1841-1865The database also has: 1907 July A.J. Allom's marginal note in 1841-1865 E.Jollie Reminiscences (1878) (MS;ATB) 39 [Allom made the note when he borrowed the MS from Jollie's wife, and made this comment on Jollie's account of buying the best parts out of a run in 1856] This was probably about the beginning of the grid-iron process of picking the eyes out of the country, so much practised afterwards.]

>  <b>TROLLOPE</b> ... a practice which in Australia they call picking the eyes out of `the country' <i>(1874,New Zealand (various authors/titles),81</i>)

>  <b>WALLACE</b> The original settlers ... has in colonial phraseology `picked the eyes out of the country' in making their selection. <i>(1891,Rural Economy of Aust. & NZ,24</i>)

>  <b>CHAMIER</b> I took `the eyes out of it', as we say - spotted all the best patches, secured all the waterholes. <i>(1895,South Sea Siren,165</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> The runholder was also allowed a pre-emptive right of purchase on his run of eighty acres for his principal station and ten acres for each out-station. This concession ... proved later to interfere with the fuller settlement of the country, as the runholder, in his phrase, `picked the eyes out.' <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,168</i>)

>  In most districts, after a timber right had been granted, time and conditions of working would appear to have been altered more in the interest of the purchaser than of the State. Moreover, the system allowed for `picking the eyes out of' areas of standing timber and leaving the less advantageous parts to future operators. <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,567</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> .. an Australianism ... <i>(1941,NZ Slang,27</i>)

>  eye ... NZ & Austral. choicest portion, best part (pick the eyes out of the land). <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,266</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]



## eye dog
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Eye dog.- Dog that commands sheep by his eye. I daresay he acts by the same instinct that makes a pointer or setter `stand' to game. <i>(Jan 20, 1934,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Gloss. additions]

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Eye-dog: Dog that commands sheep by his eye. Presumably he acts by the same instinct that makes a pointer or a setter `stand' to game. <i>(1951,Early Canterbury Runs,367</i>)

>  <b>R.GILBERD</b> notes on Mongrel Talking of dogs: An `eye-dog' is a specialised type of header which works very quietly, never taking its eyes off the sheep it is trying to pen. Sheep men generally seem to think the dog hypnotises the sheep into being penned quietly. I believe that the fixity of its gaze and its complete concentration enable it to detect and counter any rebelliousness on the part of the sheep before the sheep have quite made up their minds. <i>(Feb 16, 1952,Personal Communication</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> But the plain working dog is disappearing. The eye dog is taking his place. And if the eye dog can handle merino sheep on high country better than his predecessor, he must be good - very, very good. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,43</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> Eye Dog: Heading dog which controls sheep by a concentrated stare. <i>(1959,Tall Tussock,124</i>)

>  <b>SHARPE</b> Such dogs are often described as `strong-eyed' or `eye' dogs. <i>(1962,Country Occasions,97</i>)

>  ... someone would always trot out the old theory about eye dogs being biters. <i>(1966,NZ Short Stories,128,1976 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Middleton `Saving the Breed')]

>  <b>HASCOMBE</b> The one in the middle's a header, or an eye-dog he's sometimes <i>(1969,Down and Almost Under,81</i>)

>  eye dog n. N.Z. a dog trained to control sheep by staring fixedly at them. Also called: strong-eye dog. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,394</i>) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 266; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 173]]

>  Eye-dog: Same as a heading dog. One that shows `eye'. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,24</i>)

>  You need a good dog - an eye dog, because a huntaway will arouse the farmer from slumber. <i>(Dec 5, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),29</i>)

>  ...he's the best eye dog I've ever owned. Got eyeballs like dart-board bull's eyes. Paralyses the sheep with them, stops them dead. <i>(2003,Huia 5: Contemp. Maori Fiction,236</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a sheepdog that controls sheep by fixing them with an hypnotic stare [a kind of heading dog].

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Eye dog, dog that commands sheep by his eye. <i>(1951,Early Canterbury Runs,376</i>)

>  <b>BEACH</b> Boke was bred from a top eye dog out of a three quarter Boxer bitch ... <i>(1993,Over 30 Years Pig Hunting,33</i>)

>  <b>KERR</b> Heading dogs (also called eye dogs) work by stalking, the threat of ambush and going in for the kill. [plain eye dog; seeing eye dog] <i>(2000,High Times in the High Country,135</i>)



## eye roll
 
 <i>v.</i> roll the eyes

>  When he first told me about Te Araroa I was eye rolling and thinking oh yes, yes, yes and wishing he'd shut up. <i>(Mar 2004,North & South,74</i>)



## eye trouble
 
 <i>n.</i> have you got eye trouble, `why are you staring?' [prisoners']

>  As I walked from the yard into the day room [at Lake Alice Mental Hospital] for the first time a young guy said to another: `Have you got eye trouble?' `Yeah,' he said. `I'll fix it for you,' said the young guy - and smashed the other patient in the eyes with his fists. `Eye trouble' is a common expression in Lake Alice and prisons. It `afflicts' those who stare at others and it results in eyes being punched. <i>(Jul 10, 1984,NZ Truth,15</i>)



## eye-blight
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) ophthalmia of cattle.

>  Ophthalmia of cattle. This disease, also known as 'pink-eye' or 'eye-blight' is most contagious ... <i>(Jan 13, 1955,NZ Farmer,6</i>)



## eye-clip
 
 <i>v.</i> Farming: to clip the wool over-growing asheep's eyes.

>  (Acland Gloss.) Eye-clip: To cut the wool away from round a sheep's eyes. If this is not done, the wool, especially on merinos is apt to grow over the and make the sheep wool-blind. Some people speak of eye-clipping as Winking. (G.P.W.; C.) <i>(Oct 14, 1933,Press, The,15</i>)

>  Lambs ... while ewes are being shorn ... are being wigged or eye-clipped ... Through eye clipping, wool blindness is avoided; a wool-blind sheep is seriously hampered on run country in its search for feed and consequently does not thrive. <i>(Oct 16, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,349</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> Our next job was to eye clip the lambs; for many of them were wool-blind. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,54</i>)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> We had to eye clip the hoggets on the Station block, and it was getting into May. So after the eye clipping was finished we spent about a week getting our horses shod, and most of the men were paid off. <i>(1968,Remembered Trails,44</i>)

>  eye-clip ... NZ & Austral. clip wool round eyes of (sheep), wool so clipped. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,266</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]



 
 <i>v.</i>

>  <b>NEWTON</b> The yards were right alongside the hut and with all hands on the job the eye-clipping wasn't much trouble. After they were eye-clipped we shifted about half of them further up the Esk. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,159</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to clip the wool from around the top of the head and eyes of sheep. (See [eye-wig](../E#eye-wig))

>  <b>MOWAT</b> Four of us went out 7 finished mustering station block, other four eyeclipping. <i>(May 28, 1908,Alexander Mowat Diaries</i>)

>  <b>MOWAT</b> Finished the wethers then eye-clipped the hoggets. <i>(Aug 1, 1912,Alexander Mowat Diaries</i>)

>  The ewes and hoggets are button-holed and eye-clipped before the rams go out in April, and crutched in August, just before lambing. <i>(Oct 9, 1952,NZ Farmer,14</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> Our next job was to eye clip the lambs, for many of them were wool-blind. After the eye-clipping came the dagging. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,54</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> At the time of my visit the boys .. were eye-clipping wethers ... <i>(1975,Sixty Thousand on the Hoof,211</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) wool that is shorn from sheep's eyes and head. (See [wigging; eye-clipping](../W#wigging;-eye-clipping))

>  <b>BARTON</b> Descriptions of style: crutchings. good: uniform in length, no eye clips. Average: may contain .. no eye-clips. Inferior: Free from eye-clips and daggy wool. <i>(1972,Sheepfarming Annual 1972,108</i>)

>  <b>MAF</b> Eye clips: Trade term for wool removed from the sides of the face at crutching which though soft contains a high proportion of kemps. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,55</i>)

>  <b>SQUIRE</b> Eyeclips - (E/C) Wool from the head of a sheep. [eye-clip] <i>(1991,A to Z Glossary of Wool Terms,19</i>)



## eye-clipping
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) (See [eye-clip v](../E#eye-clip-v))

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Merino sheep ... were not handled so often for eye-clipping and so on. <i>(1930,Early Canterbury Runs,First Series vi 18</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> I was wanted with my dogs for the spring eye-clipping. <i>(1947,Wayleggo,45</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> EYECLIPPING: Some of the finer woolled breeds of sheep at times grow too much wool on their heads. They become `wool blind,' and it is necessary to `eye clip' them. <i>(1949,High Country Days,195,1950 ed.</i>)

>  Lambs ... while ewes are being shorn ... are being wigged or eye-clipped ... Through eye clipping, wool blindness is avoided; a wool-blind sheep is seriously hampered on run country in its search for feed and consequently does not thrive. <i>(Oct 16, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,349</i>)

>  <b>McLEOD</b> Several years ago we mustered in a mob of wethers for eye-clipping in June. <i>(1951,High Country,40</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Eye-clipping: Sheep that become wool-blind (i.e. grow so much wool on their heads they cannot see) must periodically have the wool clipped away from around their eyes. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,187</i>)

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> We had to eye clip the hoggets on the Station block, and it was getting into May. So after the eye clipping was finished we spent about a week getting our horses shod, and most of the men were paid off. <i>(1968,Remembered Trails,44</i>)

>  <b>PRESTON</b> Mustering, drafting and eye-clipping kept all hands busy. Wool - [101] blindness was always bad with merinos : this year about half the sheep had to be clipped. <i>(1978,Woolgatherers,100</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) short head-wool taken from around a sheep's eyes.

>  <b>MCLEOD</b> Crutchings, eye clippings, and late-shorn straggler wool make up a number of small lines which are a nuisance during the main shearing. <i>(1954,On the Hill,8</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the clipping of wool from a sheep's head, particularly around the eyes to avoid wool-blindness. (See [eye-wigging](../E#eye-wigging))

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Merino sheep .. were not handled so often for eye-clipping and so on. <i>(1930,Early Canterbury Runs,128</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> After the eye-clipping came the dagging. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,54</i>)

>  <b>PORTER</b> Each autumn muster Jim Cran .. would arrive to help out with the mustering, eye-clipping and dipping. <i>(1970,Under the Nor'West Arch,114</i>)

>  After weaning the flock ewes are mob-stocked through to crutching and eye-clipping in April. <i>(Dec 14, 1988,NZ Farmer,19</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>TOMLINSON</b> The Burchill musterers went home for a week's holiday and I was persuaded to help with the eye clipping muster, which started in May. <i>(1968,Remembered Trails,61</i>)



## eye-clips
 
 <i>n.</i> A trade term for wool removed from the side of the face at crutching which though soft often contains a high proportion of kemps, =topknots; =wigs. [(pl.) (woolgrowers')]

>  Eye Clips: Trade term for wool removed from the side of the face at crutching which though soft often contains a high proportion of kemps. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,55</i>)



## eye-drop
 
 <i>n.</i> [child marble players')]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> There was no football. It arrived suddenly in 1885. We played the town boys at cricket, sometimes using solid wooden bats. There were few spring-handed bats about. At lunchtime we frequently played Prisoner's Base. We played marbles, Ring, and Eye-drop with taws, glassies, stonies, and agots. <i>(1981,History of Children's Play,139</i>) [NOTES: 1870-1885 Grovetown in Sutton-Smith History of Children's Play]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> In the nineteenth century the ring game seems to have been the most popular marble game. (In more recent years marble games with holes have had the greater role.) There was Big Ring, in which a circle a yard or more in diameter was drawn on the ground; there was the mullibar variation, in which an outsize taw was catapulted amongst the marbles in this circle. There were Little Ring, Liney, Follows, Follow the Taw, Holey, and then, on a less important scale, Butcher's Board, in which the marbles were bowled through the holes in a board, Eye-Drop, Knock-Backs, Leggings-Out (or Leggin's-Out), Span-'Em, and Backits. The last was more usually a girls' game. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,174</i>)



## eye-opener
 
 <i>n.</i> a conman?

>  The population of Tuapeka has been increased if not honoured, by a visit from a select party of eye-openers from the other colonies, who practice the lucrative game of the thimble, fleecing the green ones to the tune of [pounds]5 a bet and, failing custom at that, Monte for a change ... <i>(Oct 22, 1861,Gabriel's Gully</i>) [NOTES: 1861 Oct 22 Gabriel's Gully [early NZ newspaper] in 1971 Manson I Take up My Pen 87]



## eye-wig
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) wool from around the eyes and the head of a sheep (See [eye-wig v](../E#eye-wig-v))

>  Eyewigs were a totally different type of wool to normal crutchings. <i>(1985,NZ Shearer,12 Autumn/Winter 48</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> eye clip @v.

>  Each ewe ... is eye-wigged if necessary. <i>(Apr 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,387/3</i>) [NOTES: [OED2]]

>  <b>BOWEN</b> ... many of our sheep have become wool-blind, i.e. wool grows over their eyes so that they cannot see. These sheep are usually eye-wigged at crutching time. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,64</i>)

>  eye-wig NZ & Austral. = eye-clip. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,266</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to remove the wool from around the eyes and head of sheep [to prevent woolblindness and blindness where seeds become stuck in wool around the eyes, inflaming and infecting them.]

>  Each ewe .. is eye-wigged if necessary. <i>(Apr 30, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,387</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> These sheep are usually eye-wigged at crutching time. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,64</i>)

>  He did not eye-wig his Merinos last season and some of them came in wool blind. <i>(Dec 14, 1988,NZ Farmer,26</i>)



## eye-wigging
 
 <i>n.</i> removing wool from the head of a sheep.

>  <b>BOWEN</b> Sheep that require eye-wigging have the top-knot removed, and wool is also taken from below the eyes if required. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,64</i>)

>  Removal of wool from the head (eye wigging) ... <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,49</i>)

>  Clean heads and points means they [sc. Coopworths] do not require eye-wigging and they move well. <i>(Sep 30, 2004,Dominion Post,C10</i>)



## eyebright
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Euphrasia Dyeri. </i>

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1908,AJHR,C-12</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> Spec. use of eyebright <i>Euphrasia officinalis</i> for local spp.

>  Amongst the known kinds of plants [in Queen Charlotte Sound are common and rough Bindweed, ... Eyebright [fni Euphrasia. [ed. [There are a number of species; Euphrasias are generally alpines.]]] and groundsel. <i>(Feb 1777,Cook's Journals,3/80,1967 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1777 Feb Anderson's Journal in Cook Journals (1967)]

>  <b>POLACK</b> <i>Knot-grass, brambles, eye-bright, groundsell</i>, and minor herbaceous vegetation, supposed to be solely indigenous to New Zealand, abound throughout the country. <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),1/295</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> <i>Euphrasia zealandica </i> <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The</i>)

>  <i>Euphrasia Monroi </i> <i>(1885,NZ Country Journal,Vol I 1877-XXII 1898</i>)

>  <b>HETLEY</b> <i>Euphrasia Monroi </i> <i>(1888,Native Flowers of NZ</i>)

>  <b>HETLEY & RAOUL</b> <i>E. Monroi </i> <i>(1889,Fleurs Sauvages</i>)

>  The process would be closely analogous to that exhibited by the native sandalwood (<i>Fusanus cunninghamii</i>), and the `eyebright' (<i>Euphrasia cuneata</i>) ... <i>(1895,TrNZI,28/497</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> (And) <i>Euphrasia Cockayniana </i>... (And)<i>Euphrasia Monroi </i> <i>(1910,NZ Plants & Their Story</i>)

>  [Waimakariri, Canterbury High Country] Scabweeds cover the rocks ... The downy seeds of thescabweeds fills [sic] the hollows like swansdown ... and dotted in profusion amidst the tussock are magnificent Mt Cook lillies [sic], Eyebright and gentian all the way to the hut ... <i>(1978,Manawatu Tramping Club Jubilee,89</i>)

>  The innocent-looking eyebrights (<i>Euphrasia</i>) have also solved their nutrient problem, not by murder, but by theft. Tapping into their neighbour's roots, these floral vampires steal liquid nourishment. <i>(Sep 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 53/47</i>)

>  The innocent-looking eyebrights (<i>Euphrasia</i>) have also solved their nutrient problem, not by murder, but by theft. Tapping into their neighbour's roots, these floral vampires steal liquid nourishment. <i>(Sep 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 53/47</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> A small alpine herb; a mountain plant with yellow and [or] white flowers. <i>Euphrasia monroi</i>, an alpine plant chiefly [??typically] of the mountains of Nelson andMarlborough.

>  <i>alpine eyebright</i> n: a showy New Zealand perennial herb (<i>Euphrasia monroi</i>) with yellow and white flowers. <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,62</i>)

## eyes out
 
 <i>phr.</i> at top speed, flat out

>  My horse turning to [sic] quickly while I was going eyes out, fell and rowled oaver [sic]. <i>(Jan 8, 1862,Chudleigh Diary,63,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ROBERTS</b> You weren't travelling `eyes out' were you? <i>(1863,Diary,28</i>) [NOTES: [OED2 1895 J Roberts Diary 28 J. Roberts date is 1895: check this]]

>  <b>HAWDON</b> We went `eyes out' to catch up. <i>(1907,New Zealanders & the Boer War,185</i>) [NOTES: 1900 letter in 1907 Hawdon New Zealanders and Boer War]

>  De Blo is toiled like a demon in the Melrose forward ranks, and Jack Spencer, King, Bill Adams and Sullivan went `eyes' out after a win which didn't come their way, however. <i>(Aug 18, 1906,NZ Truth,61/2/6</i>)

>  <b>PASCOE</b> Musterers go `eyes out' to keep the sheds fed with sheep. <i>(Apr 24, 1945,NZ Geographer</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,177</i>) [NOTES: quoting Butler FY]

>  eyes out NZ & Austral. colloq. at top speed, flatout. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,266</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  <b>McGILL</b> eyes out as fast as possible; a variation of the Australian convict era phrase `to go eyes out', to work very hard; eg `Okay, lads, eyes out and we'll be through this by three.' ANZ. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,43</i>)



## eyes-off operator
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  With this turnstyle the eyes-off operator was not fully employed. <i>(May 1972,NZ Dairy Exporter,00</i>) [NOTES: Check pagination]
